---
title: "Why I Left Linux"
date: 2008-06-22
forum: Testimonials &amp; Experiences
---

### Post by helloyo on 2008-06-22
I’m writing this thread to show how someone like you moved from being exclusively Linux for years to being exclusively Microsoft. This isn’t because I love Microsoft, or hate Linux, but because I assessed my option and chose which one suited me most. 

A quick bit of history: I started using Linux with Red Hat 9 and was exclusively Linux not long after. Since then I have tried every major distribution and some of the smaller ones. Where I remained for the last couple years was on Ubuntu due to its large user base, professional releases and support. 

Recently I realised I was being too fundamentalist about many things in my life. I was very much set in my ways and was not open to different options. I discovered that I was doing this and made a lot of changes. Then I realised that I hadn’t given Windows a chance in many years. Linux was great and had been a lot of fun, but I wanted to spend more time using my computer than tinkering with it. 

So I took the plunge, wiped Linux and installed my old copy of Windows XP for a trial. Were there issues? Absolutely, but as a Linux user they were no problem to solve. Not only that, but learning the quirks wouldn’t benefit me for one 6 month release cycle, but for several years and on countless computers. I had become clueless about the vast majority of computer systems out there and needed to relearn a lot of it. 

So after years I played around on the seven year old Windows XP. I installed Windows versions of Firefox, Skype, Flash and Opera. They all worked better. Firefox supported more plugins, Skype was a generation ahead, Flash didn’t have Z-Index issues and Opera didn’t have a mismatched menu bar. I then installed software (trials) that I hadn’t used in years: Office 2007, Photoshop CS3 and some games. Their capabilities were phenomenal. Of course they’re not free and cost a fortune to produce, but that’s the point. They are a better product for a higher price. 

I soon took the plunge and bought Vista. It sure isn’t perfect, but it is still fantastic due to what it enables me to do. I can still support free software, but when it is advantageous for me to do it. I use Firefox because it is better, not because it is open source. That is also why I use Office 2007 over OpenOffice.org. 

 I love computers and marvelling at the new things that software can do. I used to think that the best way to pursue this hobby was with Linux but now I’m not so sure. Just playing around with the free, integrated voice recognition in Vista demonstrates that.

Linux is a massive testament to collaboration and what it can achieve. All I’m saying is keep your options open. Sure it costs more money, but considering how much time some of us spend on our computers it may be worth the investment.

---

You are entitled to your opinion! Best of Luck!

And IMHO it is not nessecary that you spend time in Linux more in tinkering it with. In fact arguably in Windows you do that: Driver Installation, Antivirus, Antispyware and what not.
In fact using Windows only makes you close minded and not look at the options. In fact if you use Linux you will keep keep open the choices and will use alternate options if they are indeed better.

P.S.: I seriously doubt you ever used Linux for a long time. No experienced Linux user in right mind would completely wipe out Linux to keep Windows. He/She will dual boot for sure.

### Post by HyperHacker on 2008-06-22
Personally I agree that a lot of free/open-source software doesn't quite compare to the commercial stuff (There are filters in Photoshop CS2 - let alone CS3 - that I'd like to see in Gimp), but I feel it's a fair trade for the extra features Linux has, such as Compiz, easy automatic updates and software catalogs with no annoying EULAs, the /dev system letting you use standard file functions for what would be a massive pain on Windows, greater security, etc. Also, FOSS doesn't generally do annoying things like have advertisements (ahem, Nero) or dump shortcuts everywhere without permission (ahem, damn near everything, especially Nero). Finally, no DRM, no activation, no licensing crap to deal with, just install and run.

Fortunately, we have Wine. :P

### Post by lisati on 2008-06-22
[SIZE=2]Different installations suit different people.[/SIZE]

[LIST]
[*]I use Ubuntu Feisty (7.04) on my laptop because (a) it suits me to do so, (b) I've got it working pretty much how I want it. Still a couple of minor annoyances, but too insignificant to worry about.
[*]I keep XP on my desktop because (a) it suits me to do so, and (b) I'm too lazy to get the video editing software I use working with Wine
[*]As I type, I'm part way through installing Kubuntu as a dual-boot on my desktop (a) because I want to (in the interests of doing some learning) and (b) it will be a safe alternative to Windows if someone wants to use my desktop
[/LIST]

### Post by madjr on 2008-06-22
Just a word of [COLOR="Red"]**caution**[/COLOR] (and some stuff you have to know if you're moving into windows)

before **entering passwords** on websites, make sure you check and remove all the **spyware** / malware / **[COLOR="Purple"]keyloggers[/COLOR]** / **worms** / trojans.

also, don't make purchases with **credit cards or paypal accounts**.

and **don't enter any personal information**.

check **usb sticks** for **viruses** every time. But some viruses can't be detected or cleaned...

remember to **defrag** (once or twice monthly).

try not to **download** files/attachments, many have undetectable trojans horses.

oh and get a **[COLOR="Purple"]registry cleaner[/COLOR]** or you'll have lots of **garbage there that will slow down your system**.

At least on every boot you should check if any **weird/suspicious processes are running in the background** and kill them. They could be sending personal info to the net, etc. Many times they are cleverly disguised.

Oh and **antiviruses/spyware removers may from time to time [COLOR="Red"]insert[/COLOR] Viruses and spyware in your system** ([COLOR="Sienna"]**this not a joke**[/COLOR])  they usually insert them so you **keep it** and say *"WOW this antivirus found a ton of viruses the "other brand" didn't"*. **It's a cruel business, they create many viruses** so they can keep you using their software.

You may want to keep linux around for all this stuff and **peace of mind** (aka dual-boot).

i do this for a living and i know **windows is a battle field**

good luck

---

### Post by BigSilly on 2008-06-22
Great first post, helloyo. I won't quote it, but I agree with a lot of the sentiment. 

I've had a year exclusively Linux now myself too, and have recently begun to feel I ought to give XP another go alongside my Linux. I'm torn though, because I can still remember the pain in the **** that it can be. Constantly checking for viruses and spyware, registry cleaners and defragging etc. You start to feel (however misguided) that you're a bit invulnerable to these problems in Linux. You don't worry as much, and I'm not sure I want to worry again like I used to. 

I've yet to come to a decision (somebody talk me out of it!), but I am leaning to installing it again. So much so I've started downloading free security progs and utilities and whatnot. What a load of faff. I have a feeling it won't be long before I'm back though. I'd be installing OpenOffice.Org in XP too - that's one piece of software we love here. And Firefox, Thunderbird...does Amarok work in XP? I'd have that too. Why am I doing this again? :D All my fave stuff is in Linux!

We'll see. I've not done it yet. Good luck yourself though.

### Post by Canis familiaris on 2008-06-22
> **gameryoshi600 said:**
> This is why I am leaving linux too. I am tired of tinkering with my computer, I want to use it for what its meant for.
I suggest you one thing. Take out some time and try out both Operating system.
In Windows calculate how much time is taken to install essential hardware, essential programs like Office, Firefox, etc. and security updates, security programs. Do the corresponding in Linux.
Whichever takes lesser time is the one which needs less tinkering would be your OS. 

Here is my experience of using both as the sole OS:

Initial Configuration:
----------------------

Configuring Windows XP
----------------------
Installation: 25 minutes
Driver Installation through driver CD: 35 minutes
Installing Security Programs: 30 minutes
Installing Firefox/Office/VLC: 45 minutes
Finalizing the Installation: 10 minutes

TOTAL TIME: 120 minutes (2 hours)

Bootup Time (fresh Windows): 1 min 5 sec
Bootup Time (After config): 1 min 30 sec

REMARKS: It is easy to use and I have tons of well known programs to use. Experience would be sweet for first few weeks, then as I use it more it slows down even after defrag. 
I use a pen drive then:
Some virus or spyware attacks so much so that I am unable to browse Windows again. I have to bootup in diagnostic mode (MSCONFIG) and install AVG's latest version and scan for viruses. It removes the virus and then I can surf the internet again. I have to disable autorun and whenever a pen drive is inserted I have to virus scan it.
Then I have to update each and every app manually and that takes time as well.
Then after few month Windows does not boot at all and I have to re-install it.
Productivity: 60%
Knowledge Gained: Zero

Configuring Ubuntu 
------------------
Installation: 15 minutes
Driver Installation through driver CD: 0 minutes
Installing Security Programs: 0 minutes
Installing Firefox/Office/VLC: 5 minutes
Installing restricted extras: 5 minutes
Installing kubuntu-desktop: 15 minutes
Finalizing the Installation: 10 minutes

TOTAL TIME: 55 minutes

Installing ATI fglrx and compiz*: 5 days

TOTAL TIME (After ATI Config): 55 minutes + 5 days

Bootup Time: 1 min 20 sec

Deciede for yourself what is the total time. Personally I am inclined to 55 minutes because it was time I took to make Ubuntu usable as I did Windows.
If I include similar patches to configure Windows I suppose I can say it took weeks to configure.
ANd guess what I did not spend complete 5 days to configure fglrx and compiz.

REMARKS: It is easy to use and especially easy for me since I have been using Ubuntu for more than 1 year 6 months. I have compiz for eye candy and the OS feels zippy. I cannot use the "well known programs" but I have alternatives. I cannot use OCR. But I do not have to scan for viruses, spyware, etc. It crashes rarely, atleast not as much as Windows. I can browse Internet freely.

Productivity: 65%
Knowledge gained: Quite a lot. I learned a lot about X, ati, fglrx, etc.

So I use Ubuntu. I suppose I'm not a freak or fundamentalist.

### Post by manfer on 2008-06-23
If you want to improve university reports, please, learn latex.

If you want a computer for dummies, please, buy a MAC (with a desktop so so easy to use, but with a robust UNIX like system in its insides which gives you all the power on its terminal, and without all those security issues on a Win platform).

If you want gaming, please, buy a console.

If you like to learn everything about computers with a robust UNIX like system and its power, at the lowest cost, use a linux distro. Yes, sometimes it is a headache to get all hardware working (the hardware manufactures are still not developing linux drivers in a lot of cases; I can't even imagine the effort of developing a driver without the hardware specs; we have to be so grateful to all those people on the linux community developing them); yes it is a headache when you test things that goes beyond your knowledge and you break the system (linux requires a more learning effort and a lot of patient); but after all, it worths the effort.

The only advantage of Windows compared with its competitors is the huge among of soft in that platform (as an example gaming on computers). But the security issues and low performance worths it?. Every time they develop a new version it needs more hardware specifications, in my opinion excessive.

And as all others says, you have always the chance to have both linux and Windows in the same computer if you want (I do myself in the PC).

*Note: For those Gimp vs photoshop comments. Gimp is a very good free application but for professional use I think it is still not enough.*

### Post by Tridion2000 on 2008-06-23
No idea what trolling is so I can't say if I am doing it or not. Explain trolling and I'll let you know.

You know what, I'll be a little more balanced in my argument. Yes I think Linux is a waste of my time. That's a personal opinion and I am just as entitled to it as you guys are on thinking it is the best thing since sliced bread. Fair play to you.

In Windows, I just plug in my 360 controller and it works. Simple. Plug it into Ubuntu and there is a whole long bloody thread on how to get it to work. Recompiling this, recompiling that, etc, etc, etc. Plug my iPod Touch into Windows and it just works. Plug it into Ubuntu and read half a dozen threads on how you can't get it to work without Jailbreaking it. Sorry I have better things to do than waste my time with this jailbreaking rubbish. Things either work or they don't. Period.

I have many more examples of the headaches of using Ubuntu.

But now the flip side of the coin. I have found Ubuntu to be better for some things. Bittorrent works a LOT better. None of this restriction on the number of half open TCP/IP connections, etc. VMWare and Virtualbox seem to work a lot more efficiently in Linux than they do in Windows especially in Vista. After a few headaches, connecting my laptop to an external display is actually a lot easier than in Windows.

However, for me personally, the cons far outweight the pros and I find myself wanting to go back to Windows. I am not the kind of personal who can be arsed dual booting. That is just a hassle. I know some will disagree but I just think it is too much effort.

My comments yesterday I mainly meant but were also slightly fuelled by my final frustration of getting things I need working in Ubuntu and deciding to give up while I still have some hair left. Sorry but for me Windows works, does what I need out of the box and I don't need to start recompiling kernels and all that crap. Yes I pay for that privelege and understand why some people might take a free option instead. Each to their own.

### Post by Dlizard on 2008-06-23
I've been in a changing process from Windows to Ubuntu for the last four months. It has not been always as easy as some linux literature preaches. In the process I faced lots of problems, including losing some precious data in a suppose "simple dual Linux/Windows installation". 

I have no doubt that the difficulties I am facing are in part due to the fact I am not a "computer person" ou a "computer geek". In other words, I use computers primarily because I need them as instruments to do my job. 

Despite all the problems I don't intend to go back to Windows. MS is far from being perfect either, and, most important, my decision is strongly based on a philosophical world view. 

However, I must say that the person who started this thread has a point. Many things work very well in Ubuntu, but others can be very complicated and  arcane for an ordinary computer user.

As an example,for the last two days I've been trying to find a backup mechanism for Ubuntu similar to the one I used in Windows. With MS I used Nero. Despite all the (justified) criticism to that program it allowed me to do a very simple thing: to backup my files in a compressed form and DIVIDE the whole thing in sequential DVDs. Despite all the talk I've  seen in the forums about "how easy" it is to backup my Ubuntu files, I cannot find anything easy about it. Following those advises I made a backup yesterday that was too big to fit in any DVD. I couldn't divide it in many DVDs. I saw some other methods, but they look like discussions among black-belt masters in the martial art of SUDO. No way a begginer non-geek can understand. Finally, I am inclined to buy Nero for Linux. 

So, to finish this long message, I think Linux/Ubuntu is a wonderful path on which I intend to stay. However, in my opinion there is still an underestimation of the problems I mentioned above. I think that maybe some commercial alternatives would be welcome to Ubuntu, mainly because it would make the ordinary-computer-user feel more comfortable and secure, since there would be a measure of accountability on the other side (the program suppliers). 

Of course such a commercial choice should be limited in order to avoid destroying the whole Linux/Ubuntu philosophy. But that is another story...

---

### Post by OmniCloud on 2008-06-23
> **Tridion2000 said:**
> No idea what trolling is so I can't say if I am doing it or not. Explain trolling and I'll let you know.

You know what, I'll be a little more balanced in my argument. Yes I think Linux is a waste of my time. That's a personal opinion and I am just as entitled to it as you guys are on thinking it is the best thing since sliced bread. Fair play to you.

In Windows, I just plug in my 360 controller and it works. Simple. Plug it into Ubuntu and there is a whole long bloody thread on how to get it to work. Recompiling this, recompiling that, etc, etc, etc. Plug my iPod Touch into Windows and it just works. Plug it into Ubuntu and read half a dozen threads on how you can't get it to work without Jailbreaking it. Sorry I have better things to do than waste my time with this jailbreaking rubbish. Things either work or they don't. Period.

I have many more examples of the headaches of using Ubuntu.

But now the flip side of the coin. I have found Ubuntu to be better for some things. Bittorrent works a LOT better. None of this restriction on the number of half open TCP/IP connections, etc. VMWare and Virtualbox seem to work a lot more efficiently in Linux than they do in Windows especially in Vista. After a few headaches, connecting my laptop to an external display is actually a lot easier than in Windows.

However, for me personally, the cons far outweight the pros and I find myself wanting to go back to Windows. I am not the kind of personal who can be arsed dual booting. That is just a hassle. I know some will disagree but I just think it is too much effort.

My comments yesterday I mainly meant but were also slightly fuelled by my final frustration of getting things I need working in Ubuntu and deciding to give up while I still have some hair left. Sorry but for me Windows works, does what I need out of the box and I don't need to start recompiling kernels and all that crap. Yes I pay for that privelege and understand why some people might take a free option instead. Each to their own.Why you didn't do this in the first place I don't know. 

Trolling, is when you don't really care about anyone else's replies, you simply want to express your view (usually negative on the subject matter) and annoy the living crap out of anyone (everyone) that doesn't agree. I'm sure there's a definition you can find by googling though--but that's the gist. 

I can't relate, one, because I don't play games on PC (PS3 & MGS4 OMG!!! Awesomeness!!!:guitar:) I think I had a regular Ipod a while back and didn't have any trouble getting the songs off it at least--can't really remember. 

However, it's obvious these newer tech is only going to work with what it's designed for buddy-Windows. 

For YOU the cons far outweight the pros. For many, it's the exact opposite. I can customize my desktop the ExACT way I want it. It's more stable, runs faster, more personal etc..the list for each individual is different. 

You know the reason I switched over to Linux--because it sounded like a cool idea! The reason I stayed--was because I could setup my desktop with so many different styles--it's become my little hobby. 

Some people don't care how everything looks, if it's a little faster, or whatever--the point is, you don't need linux in the first place. It's here, it's free, it's up to each user to make what you will out of it. 

Some use it as just a toy, many as there main desktop, some dual-boot. It's whatever. 

When you complain about stuff--it's kind of annoying. 

1) Linux is a free choice OS with many different flavors, there are OS's designed specifically for users that just want things to work

2) Linux is not a Windows replacement. There are many apps/devices designed to exclusively work with Windows. 

3) Some things has open-sourced alternatives, some things don't. No one forces you to try Linux. It's just something cool that maybe your friend, recommended. 

I understand there are fanboys on all sides, and some do hype Linux up like it's the replacement for all your problems. That can be 100% true for some, and complete BS for others. 

Notice, how all of my points are very subjective/open-ended/two-fold. This is the philosophy of using open-sourced stuff. You make of it, what you will--nuff said. 

This is why so many have embraced it, and it will continue to grow. You know, Mac is based on Unix, maybe another one of those will spring out with some type of Linux distro. However, until that happens, you shouldn't expect everything to work in Linux like it does in Windows. 

I mean come'on an X360 controller??! What the hell is that man? Did you honestly think that was gonna just zip by flawlessly in Linux? Gaming is just getting up and running on Mac let alone GNU/Linux bro.

Like I said, I recommend a Mac for you--or maybe Vista. It seems you wanted something better, but still have things that rely heavily on Windows. Unless your gonna get down in the dirt and figure them out for yourself, it's just not gonna fly with Linux. There's but so much hand-holding here, and with good reason--you didn't pay for damn thing. 

Mac is IMO, prettier, more stable (than Vista anyway) easier to use, more customizable, and growing in user base. (which means stuff like Xbox controller and of course Apple's Ipod, won't be a problem) Your gonna pay a grip--but I don't think that's a problem if you get what you want. 

Try it out at Best buy or something bro--there pretty cool. Definitely expensive though. 

btw--take a look at the screenshot thread/popular apps thread. Yeah--there are something that you can do ONLY ON Linux/Unix platforms too.

Some people use it just because they can spin there 3D desktop around lol.

### Post by jasontu on 2008-06-23
I can completely understand this sentiment.  As a recent migrant to Linux, it can be frustrating to feel like you won't ever have anything just as you like it.  That's part of being the cutting edge.  If you want to be ahead of the curve in terms of an OS experience, you're going to have to expect experiments to occasionally fail.  It's a pain, but that's the price you pay.

Definitely dual boot to XP for games if you must.  Personally, I have been lucky enough to find that most of my favorite games run perfectly well on Wine or natively in Linux.

Now that I have tried Linux (for about the 4th time) and have found a relatively painless functional distribution, I think I will always have at least one box running Ubuntu in my home.

I would suggest that you at least dual-boot, simply because you never know when Windows will get its next blaster-worm or super-virus that will cripple everyone else.

XP is a good OS if you know how to take care of it and be careful with what you do.  My wife runs XP on her PC, but I have no worries about it.  She uses the same programs, visits the same safe websites over and over again.  There's adware to remove, etc., but it works.  Plus we have a box we can connect some of the less linux-friendly hardware to.

As for the geek-OS comments flying around... I would say that I  am willing to be a geek for a while to tweak Ubuntu to where I need it to be.  I'm not going to take it up as a full-time job.  I have NEVER recompiled anything, much less a kernel...lol  

If someone spends all their time tweaking their Ubuntu installation they may be demanding a bit much from the OS.  

You can do a lot with Linux, but you can't have it write an opera for you or foretell the future.

I think some people get so taken with how much you can do with this system that they start to demand more from it than they would ever demand from Windows.

### Post by leve4919 on 2008-06-23
Not yet... After many-many years of loyal Windows service, I decided to give Linux a try. (As a matter of fact 10 or so years ago I had RH installed and configured, but did not like it). Installed Ubuntu 8.04 and was astonished! It was like a talking dog :) It just worked. And, because I had nothing in it IT WAS FAST. But then I started using it and found that not all is that smooth as it looked. 

1. With no "declaration of war" and with no actions on my side (but an automatic update), it stopped automatically connecting to the wireless network at boot time. I checked all relevant files/places (and google). All is absolutely kosher, but nothing helped. So I have to connect manually by pretending that I modified the wireless settings and only then it connects.

2. I was very disappointed when I found, that just received payment transaction ID which I copied to the clipboard (with an intention to paste it into a document), was gone when I closed the browser. Google explained, that the clipboard is crippled by design. To fix the problem I installed "clipboard-daemon" only to find that it:
a. works in 50% cases
b. does not cover all applications (do not want to go into details. I filed a bug).
Somebody suggested to install "Klipper". Ironically enough it works.

3. Evolution (the stock "Outlook replacement") silently crashed when I was changing some options. When I restarted it, half of the standard folders were non accessible (the best was "trash"). To fix it I had to remove all e-mail accounts, delete the "leftover messages" and add accounts again. This time all was almost fine, but I could not synchronize local and server folders.

4. All the sudden, "Trash" (not  Evolution's, but the "Recycle bin") stopped working. I did not have rights to purge some files. Google helped me to remove these files in  "sudo" mode, and also almost to remove the rest of the file system (almost made this mistake).

5. vi - works. Do not know if this is good or bad.

6. Installed Kdeveloper and could not compile, just created project. I do not have rights to write file into a subdir on my home directory (debug).

7. Still the system is fast.

8. Famous bash name completion does not work as well as "infamous" Windows cmd.exe name completion. In Windows  if I want I do not type anything - just tab through choices and select the one I want. In bash I have to disambiguate by typing. Wikipedia and the rest, tells that BASH'es completion is very robast and there are tons of extensions. They also tell, that windows shell has rudimentary completion facilities. Maybe they are right, but I DON'T WANT TO TYPE.

Not done with Linux yet, but I'm very close to reformating the partition. See you in 10 years.

### Post by 3rdalbum on 2008-06-24
> **leve4919 said:**
> 2. I was very disappointed when I found, that just received payment transaction ID which I copied to the clipboard (with an intention to paste it into a document), was gone when I closed the browser. Google explained, that the clipboard is crippled by design. To fix the problem I installed "clipboard-daemon" only to find that it:
a. works in 50% cases
b. does not cover all applications (do not want to go into details. I filed a bug).
Somebody suggested to install "Klipper". Ironically enough it works.

This is a rough spot with Linux, but most distributions just install an intermediary program like the one you tried. Glipper works 100% for me.

> 4. All the sudden, "Trash" (not  Evolution's, but the "Recycle bin") stopped working. I did not have rights to purge some files. Google helped me to remove these files in  "sudo" mode, and also almost to remove the rest of the file system (almost made this mistake).

No idea what happened here. It does sound more like user error, though.

> 8. Famous bash name completion does not work as well as "infamous" Windows cmd.exe name completion. In Windows  if I want I do not type anything - just tab through choices and select the one I want. In bash I have to disambiguate by typing. Wikipedia and the rest, tells that BASH'es completion is very robast and there are tons of extensions. They also tell, that windows shell has rudimentary completion facilities. Maybe they are right, but I DON'T WANT TO TYPE.

Wtf are you doing on the command-line then?

On your Ubuntu installation, do the following into the terminal:

```
apt-ca <tab> sh <tab> network-m <tab>
```

You'll find "apt-cache show network-manager" in your terminal.
Windows can auto-complete only program names. Bash can auto-complete parameters to a program. How powerful's that?

### Post by Haulnbones on 2008-06-24

I've read where some people flamed Windows for their lack of security, but that's just damned foolish speak. Should a version of Linux ever defeat the Giant, and produce an operating system that will support multiple platforms with little or no driver issues and be exceptionally user friendly then they would attain that target. Then we'd find out exactly how secure Linux is. I'll bet money that it's not all it's cracked up to be. 

I'm here to experiment with Ubuntu. I've heard good things about it. I'll always be a Windows guy, but I'm open to new options. And should future builds meet the demands of the masses, I don't want to be left behind.

### Post by Canis familiaris on 2008-06-24
> **Haulnbones said:**
> And how the heck do you do that exactly? :confused:

That looks very promising to someone like me:guitar:

You need to have compiz enabled of course.

Well you have to first install Virtualbox

Go to Add/Remove;
Search for Virtualbox and install it.

Now run Virtualbox and create a guest OS by a wizard allocate space, etc. It will create a virtual machine.
Now run the virtual machine. You'll get an error message of a missing library or something. Go to Synaptic and install that package. If you're confused, post that error message here and i'll guide you.
Then install Windows in the VM and wait the installation to be complete.
Now when you are running Windows in the VM, go to Devices and install the Guest additions.
After Guest Additions is installed. In the Virtualbox menu Go To File->Preferences and in the input tab deselect "Autocapture keyboard" Now go back to he VM press Right Ctrl + F and run it in fullscreen. 
Then you could have that great Linux-Windows cube.

---

Then we'd find out exactly how secure Linux is. I'll bet money that it's not all it's cracked up to be. 

I'm here to experiment with Ubuntu. I've heard good things about it. I'll always be a Windows guy, but I'm open to new options. And should future builds meet the demands of the masses, I don't want to be left behind.
To your information Linux is the GIANT in the server world but still survives more attacks and is more secure than Windows servers.

### Post by squashpup on 2008-06-24
OS wars are stupid, because you can talk forever about which OS does what best.  It all depends on what you want to do.  I find that my computer simply performs better with Linux, but that doesn't mean I'll abandon Windows any time soon.  

Don't get me wrong.  I've used Linux for so long that I now find it very annoying when I have to boot into Windows to do something.  And, I'm not so stupid or naive as to say that I can do everything on Linux that I did on Windows.  There are simply some applications that do not run on Linux, and for those, I must boot Microsoft.

But, I prefer Linux for my day-to-day computing.  I like to tinker on my computer, but I also like for it to just work when I need it.  And it does.  The tinkering I do is voluntary, fun tinkering, instead of trying to fix the damn thing like I did so much with Microsoft.  Not offering that as proof of anything, just disappointed that the operating system that I paid good money for is so flaky that it doesn't work the way I think it should.

And, there are simply things that Microsoft can't do...for example, run a full, modern, security updated OS on the computer that I'm using now.  I am typing this from a full Xubuntu install, with LXDE as my desktop, on a 10 year old Compaq laptop...433 mhz, 196 MB RAM.  Under Windows, this computer would be unusable or a security risk...with my current setup, it's quite snappy and very usable.  

We appreciate you coming to this Linux forum to tell us you aren't going to use Linux anymore, but I'm unsure of what reaction you were looking for from us.  Use what you want.  I will too. And, I hope it doesn't offend you if I don't really care much either way.

In fact, since this is a forum primarily for Linux users, and you are now switching to Microsoft, you can now go and find a forum that is more suited to what you want to do.

Have a great day and happy computing.

### Post by kaiju on 2008-06-24
> **helloyo said:**
> Linux was great and had been a lot of fun, but I wanted to spend more time using my computer than tinkering with it.
So I took the plunge, wiped Linux and installed my old copy of Windows XP for a trial. Were there issues? Absolutely, but as a Linux user they were no problem to solve. Not only that, but learning the quirks wouldn’t benefit me for one 6 month release cycle, but for several years and on countless computers. I had become clueless about the vast majority of computer systems out there and needed to relearn a lot of it. 


you know i really spend a lot of time in front of the computer, too much even. i just love bash and the myriad tools you can use in scripts, so i read about and tinker with them a lot. but somehow - to me at least - this seems a wee bit more constructive than all the stuff my roommate has to do on an almost daily basis for his xp machine to keep working fairly well (like removal of viruses, trojans, dialers and such, sometimes fixing the windows registry and so on).

i tinker with my computer because i like to do it, i like to find new solutions to meet my purposes. and if i install twenty apps but don't really like any of them, i can just remove them all and be sure there will be no adware or corrupt registry left to deal with.

unlike with windows, a system once set up will not run only for a few months before becoming too slow or just too corrupted to be unusable. and if you're using a distro with a rolling release system, you even get the most recent versions of everything without ever needing to reinstall your os.

and as a last note, i don't know much about the vast majority of computer systems out there but it seems that the vast majority of the operating systems in use today are unix-based, so you're actually way better off with a linux background :p

.

The system is customizable on an easy way on System->Properties. Event with a right mouse click on desktop look what happens if you chose the last entry on the menu. If that is not enough is when you can start looking for something else.

The system informs you when there are updates. I personally get all those updates, I don't mind if those are for security reasons or improve soft.

Changes on the system are severely limited. You have to be root to change them. If someone without the knowledge to change things changes them anyway (a lot of us made that error more than once) and breaks system is as his own risk. But that's one of the real advantages of Linux, total control of your system, and by no reason would be nice to change it at all.

Anyway that's not something only happening on a linux system. How many have entered win registry, making a change someone told them, without knoledge, and have broken their system? Or delete a folder that must not be deleted...

The advise we must all of us simply follow is: first knowledge, then changes.

If someone does that, breaks his system and abandons linux because of it, good luck with other OS he choses.

### Post by manfer on 2008-06-24
Wow you must have a superComputer, it is so intelligent. Tell me how the hell in a folder with 1000 subfolders you get a folder name with only tab key? Let's say you want the 999 folder, do you hit tab 999 to get the name? Nooooooooooooooooooooooooooo, you type the first letters, then tab. (both bash and Windows command line)

Running an application from command line. In bash you only have to type the application name, unix filesystems have places for executables as it must be, /bin, /sbin... In Windows, each application puts its executable in its own folder, or you are adding the path to every application you install to system evirontment variable path or you first have to go to the folder containing the executable and type then its name. Or maybe you want to try if you are able to type the absolute path, c:\Pr TAB and you get "c:\Program Files", now try to go on completing.

I'm with a Home Edition XP, maybe Server Editions of Microsoft OS have more funtionality on the Command line, or even Vista, or installing utilities to do administration tasks from Microsoft. I think there's a taskkill command in any of those installations.

And you install GNU utils for Windows. Those gnu guys so nice they offers their soft to everybody no matter which platform they use (bash/vim/emacs/grep...)

It would be nice too if Microsoft do the same sometimes. At least for the more normal things they should have shared (how long linux users were not able to access THEIR OWN NTFS filesystems?)

### Post by leve4919 on 2008-06-24
Gnu utils??? Windows resource kit and administrative pack (free as in bear).

OK! This is the last time I try to explain you the difference in name completion in windows and bash. I will not talk about the directory with 1000 subdirs. Such thing should not exist in the first place. Here is the partial list of gedit source files:
gedit-document-loader.c
gedit-document-loader.h
gedit-document-saver.c
gedit-document-saver.h
gedit-documents-panel.c
gedit-documents-panel.h
gedit-encodings.c
gedit-encodings.h
gedit-encodings-option-menu.c
gedit-encodings-option-menu.h
if I want to open file in windows I type
n <tab>
it gives 
n gedit-document-loader.c
nex tab
gedit-document-loader.h
etc.
if I miss the entry, not a big deal. Can press <shift-tab>

In bash:
n <tab><tab>
gives directory listing. I have to type g<tab> to get 
n gedit-
etc.
Every time there is ambiguity I have to disambiguate the choice. More keypresses and more screen looks on the screen.

### Post by manfer on 2008-06-25
> **Anurag_panda said:**
> You are comparing Windows GUI to Linux terminal:
Does these work?
At Windows terminal:
```
PROMPT> firefox
```
Does firefox load?

And nano is quick and runs in text mode though old. Also in WIndows you have to install gvim and vim.

By debugging I meant when a program crashes a lot, can you try to find out the root cause by launching the program in commandline and seeing its output.
```
PROMPT> programname (eg. iexplore)
```
Does the CLI show any output giving hints about program execution?

Is command /? as helpful as man command?

Is there a way to terminate a program by command?
```
PROMPT> terminate iexplore
```

That is just answered I think.

For the first one. Yes you can run a program from command line in Windows (cmd shell), but, which is the difference with linux:

- When you install a program in windows, its executable is installed in its own folder. Example, if you install firefox it is installed by default in C:/Program Files/Mozilla Firefox/. So to run the program from cmd shell you have to first go to that folder then type firefox. You could add firefox path (C:/Program Files/Mozilla Firefox/) to the environment variable PATH and then you can run it from anywhere. But, are you going to add all programs paths to system environment variable PATH?

- In linux all executables are installed in /bin /sbin, ..., and those folders are just in the system variable PATH, so you can run all programs from anywhere.

-------------------------------------

The second, yes, he said there is a debugger in Windows. In the web I only saw that debugger as GUI, I didn't read too much, but for me it is sufficient answered. You can debug a program execution in Windows too, that is the important thing for comparison I think.

--------------------------------------

The third, yes you can kill a program in cmd shell if you install the administrative tools. There's a taskkill command for that purpose.

--------------------------------------

I didn't know some of the features of cmd shell but I still think bash is more powerful. I didn't know either that there is a Power shell that can be used in windows. I don't know how to run it but it is available. Here is a comparison table of shells:

[http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comparison_of_computer_shells](http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comparison_of_computer_shells)

I think the power shell is only part of Windows Server 2008, but can be installed on XP or Vista.

### Post by manfer on 2008-06-26
> **leve4919 said:**
> //In Windows I use notepad++ as text editor. Very good free application too.
On absolutely different note:
I never developed anything for linux. Where can I read about suggested development techniques? Console and UI would be nice and also distributing applications.

For console applications I think you has to select the programming language you want, then install its compiler, and if you use the language standards to code you will be able to compile and run it. There's a lot of programming languages available in repos and lots of libraries for them too.

To program a GUI you have to decide the API you would like to use and look if it is available for the programming languages you like. For example, the Gnome GUI API is GTK+, and a visual interface builder for it, Glade.

For an IDE Anjuta for C/C++ coding which integrates Glade on it.

KDE uses QT4 and an IDE for KDE development, KDevelop.

If the programming language is Java, being platform independent and with its own API, no problem with it. But java code is compiled for a virtual machine and it is not as fast as a compiled language. A good IDE, Eclipse.

Other example, python has both GTK and QT4 libraries.

This are only small notes, it is all above my knowledge, and the question is a large one. There are a lot of languages, there are a lot of compilers, there are a lot of IDEs ...

Maybe someone else could help with this or the question could be better answered in the programming forum. If you prefer any particular language I think it would be helpful to include it in the question too.

Other important data you should include is the kind of soft you intend to develop. It is not the same if you are thinking to develop open source soft, free soft but not open sourced or propietary soft. With that in mind people could explain you what you can and what you can't use in your project. Again it is above my knowledge but before starting you would have to understand the licenses of any library you use in your code. The kind of soft would affect the license you can use for it and the distribution process too.

### Post by helloyo on 2008-06-26
I thought I'd write a little follow up to show how my opinions have changed. To an extent at least. This was brought on by a couple of things. One major one is how I use the computer has changed. 

Since being back in the Windows world I have been excited to try out all the latest games. This is a big drawcard for Windows, obviously, but as much as I hate to admit it, I don't enjoy video games anymore. I've tried rekindling the flame, but that has just cost me time and money. I like reading about them, but I just don't have fun playing them anymore. 

Another issue is cost. There is some great, polished software available. At a price. As I move away from my computer based lines of work I can't justify they cost of things like Photoshop. The same goes with my mother's and girlfriend's computers. They're hardly enthusiasts, so how can I tell them fork out money for marginal benefit (in their cases at least).

Hardware support is another bonus of using a near ubiquitous operating system. Definitely a deal breaker for some, but, with the possible exception of my new TV card, I know that my hardware setup is easier in Linux. Sure my Microsoft keyboard has functioning multimedia keys. So long as you are using WMP... I've seen the pace at which hardware support has been improving in Linux so it shouldn't be a concern for most people.

Software is evolving rapidly right now with web apps and portability. This is definitely in the favour of Linux. Personally I use web apps substantially for many reasons, making operating system choice less important. As for other software which I use, here is how it compares in Linux:

Windows Media Player: Pretty looking hunk of junk that won't remove songs that don't exist or download album art. Amorak kills it.

Firefox: Hard to say if one runs better then the other. Piclens is a pretty extension available for the Windows version. I don't get the point of it though, so this is a tie for me.

Photoshop: Fantastic software, but I'm not a professional so the GIMP wins for me when price is involved.

Adobe Reader: Available in Linux, but I rarely bother installing it. Works quite well, so I'd consider it in Linux. Tie.

Skype: Much more advanced in Windows. I rarely use it, and only for landline calls, so for me its a tie.

Symantec Antivirus: Much nicer not needing this. Linux win.

Google Earth/Picasa: A few compromises on the Linux side, but it's improving.

uTorrent: Works MUCH faster then any client in Linux ever did, but I hear it is designed to work under Wine.

Microsoft Word 2007: Here is where I run into problems. I think Word 2007 is fantastic and single handled made my last few reports look great and easy to make. Maybe I could learn to use OpenOffice.org better, but in any case it gets killed here. 

Winfast PVR2: Works, but badly. Not sure how this would be on Linux though.

So there lies my problem. Show stopper apps. There are a few proprietry ones I need (may work under Wine), but Word is really holding me back. A potential solution would be to use somebody elses copy to beautify my important documents. 

I appreciate everyone's input. I may hold strong opinions but good arguments will always change them. When it comes time to put cash down I may very well be back on Linux.

---

### Post by Keyper7 on 2008-06-26
Well, it's good to hear you're changing your opinion, but for me it's simple: if you honestly feel your productivity improves with, say, Office 2007, and there's nothing equivalent in Linux, stay in Windows. Unless of course, the task your talking about is not that important to begin with.

I tend to avoid being too idealist. For me, productivity should always come first and I did hear that Office 2007 has quite neat features. OpenOffice is more than enough for me but I admit that I'm not someone who, for example, writes documents for a living, so my opinion is quite weak.

PS: even if you were a professional image editor, Linux still does fine. Wine 1.0 runs CS2 fine as far as I know. Google is even paying devs to help the wine developers on improving compatibility with Adobe apps.

### Post by thelugnut on 2008-06-27
I think that was a very good post. There is absolutely nothing wrong with people preferring MS over Linux. Just as there is nothing amiss with people preferring Linux over MS.
Costs, stability, reliability, ease of use, capabilities, and on and on.

I personally have no intention of dumping my MS Windows XP for quite  some time. It doesn't bother me that MS will discontinue support, maybe next year. I do things with MS Windows XP that I either cannot do with Ubuntu, or that takes too much time to configure. 

I have tried many Linux distributions, and they all have something to offer. Ubuntu wins out time after time in my opinion and therefore is and will be my Linux-of-choice.

So since I consider myself to be an average guy with an average computer and an average knowledge, I would venture to wager a small sum that the majority of Ubuntu users are dual booting with a MS product. And I believe that's how it should be. It's not "May the best man win", it's "We all win with multiple products for multiple reasons.".

That is all for now.:guitar:

### Post by manfer on 2008-06-27
Have you tried taskkill in your cmd command line?

Open notepad, then try the kill:

**taskkill /im notepad.exe /f**

I don't have a Pro Edition to test.

I think the administrative tools mentioned before were GUI ones and not CLI. The ones I saw are not for that purpose. Maybe the user who uses them could clarified it.

Anyway, with sysinternals tools as I said before you can do a lot of management under CLI. A PSList and a PSKill commands would be available in sysinternals process utilities.

### Post by Canis familiaris on 2008-06-27
> **manfer said:**
> Could you try something else? As I said I don't have pro edition to test.

How much information about taskkill can you obtain from system?

I suppose taskkill -help would give you a short explanation of command. Test it please.

I think nothing like man or info pages can be found on windows, so you would not be able to get any information like that. So no way to test this.

And finally in GUI help can you obtain information for that command? Test it please.

```
Microsoft Windows XP [Version 5.1.2600]
(C) Copyright 1985-2001 Microsoft Corp.

C:\tools>taskkill /?

TASKKILL [/S system [/U username [/P [password]]]]
         { [/FI filter] [/PID processid | /IM imagename] } [/F] [/T]

Description:
    This command line tool can be used to end one or more processes.
    Processes can be killed by the process id or image name.

Parameter List:
    /S    system           Specifies the remote system to connect to.

    /U    [domain\]user    Specifies the user context under which
                           the command should execute.

    /P    [password]       Specifies the password for the given
                           user context. Prompts for input if omitted.

    /F                     Specifies to forcefully terminate
                           process(es).

    /FI   filter           Displays a set of tasks that match a
                           given criteria specified by the filter.

    /PID  process id       Specifies the PID of the process that
                           has to be terminated.

    /IM   image name       Specifies the image name of the process
                           that has to be terminated. Wildcard '*'
                           can be used to specify all image names.

    /T                     Tree kill: terminates the specified process
                           and any child processes which were started by it.

    /?                     Displays this help/usage.

Filters:
    Filter Name   Valid Operators           Valid Value(s)
    -----------   ---------------           --------------
    STATUS        eq, ne                    RUNNING | NOT RESPONDING
    IMAGENAME     eq, ne                    Image name
    PID           eq, ne, gt, lt, ge, le    PID value
    SESSION       eq, ne, gt, lt, ge, le    Session number.
    CPUTIME       eq, ne, gt, lt, ge, le    CPU time in the format
                                            of hh:mm:ss.
                                            hh - hours,
                                            mm - minutes, ss - seconds
    MEMUSAGE      eq, ne, gt, lt, ge, le    Memory usage in KB
    USERNAME      eq, ne                    User name in [domain\]user
                                            format
    MODULES       eq, ne                    DLL name
    SERVICES      eq, ne                    Service name
    WINDOWTITLE   eq, ne                    Window title

NOTE: Wildcard '*' for the /IM switch is accepted only with filters.

NOTE: Termination of remote processes will always be done forcefully
      irrespective of whether /F option is specified or not.

Examples:
    TASKKILL /S system /F /IM notepad.exe /T
    TASKKILL /PID 1230 /PID 1241 /PID 1253 /T
    TASKKILL /F /IM notepad.exe /IM mspaint.exe
    TASKKILL /F /FI "PID ge 1000" /FI "WINDOWTITLE ne untitle*"
    TASKKILL /F /FI "USERNAME eq NT AUTHORITY\SYSTEM" /IM notepad.exe
    TASKKILL /S system /U domain\username /FI "USERNAME ne NT*" /IM *
    TASKKILL /S system /U username /P password /FI "IMAGENAME eq note*"

```

EDIT: The GUI help also gave the same information. I guess help for commands is somewhat like Linux.

### Post by wthex on 2008-06-29
> **helloyo said:**
> I thought I'd write a little follow up to show how my opinions have changed. To an extent at least. This was brought on by a couple of things. One major one is how I use the computer has changed. 

Since being back in the Windows world I have been excited to try out all the latest games. This is a big drawcard for Windows, obviously, but as much as I hate to admit it, I don't enjoy video games anymore. I've tried rekindling the flame, but that has just cost me time and money. I like reading about them, but I just don't have fun playing them anymore. 

Another issue is cost. There is some great, polished software available. At a price. As I move away from my computer based lines of work I can't justify they cost of things like Photoshop. The same goes with my mother's and girlfriend's computers. They're hardly enthusiasts, so how can I tell them fork out money for marginal benefit (in their cases at least).

Hardware support is another bonus of using a near ubiquitous operating system. Definitely a deal breaker for some, but, with the possible exception of my new TV card, I know that my hardware setup is easier in Linux. Sure my Microsoft keyboard has functioning multimedia keys. So long as you are using WMP... I've seen the pace at which hardware support has been improving in Linux so it shouldn't be a concern for most people.

Software is evolving rapidly right now with web apps and portability. This is definitely in the favour of Linux. Personally I use web apps substantially for many reasons, making operating system choice less important. As for other software which I use, here is how it compares in Linux:

Windows Media Player: Pretty looking hunk of junk that won't remove songs that don't exist or download album art. Amorak kills it.

Firefox: Hard to say if one runs better then the other. Piclens is a pretty extension available for the Windows version. I don't get the point of it though, so this is a tie for me.

Photoshop: Fantastic software, but I'm not a professional so the GIMP wins for me when price is involved.

Adobe Reader: Available in Linux, but I rarely bother installing it. Works quite well, so I'd consider it in Linux. Tie.

Skype: Much more advanced in Windows. I rarely use it, and only for landline calls, so for me its a tie.

Symantec Antivirus: Much nicer not needing this. Linux win.

Google Earth/Picasa: A few compromises on the Linux side, but it's improving.

uTorrent: Works MUCH faster then any client in Linux ever did, but I hear it is designed to work under Wine.

Microsoft Word 2007: Here is where I run into problems. I think Word 2007 is fantastic and single handled made my last few reports look great and easy to make. Maybe I could learn to use OpenOffice.org better, but in any case it gets killed here. 

Winfast PVR2: Works, but badly. Not sure how this would be on Linux though.

So there lies my problem. Show stopper apps. There are a few proprietry ones I need (may work under Wine), but Word is really holding me back. A potential solution would be to use somebody elses copy to beautify my important documents. 

I appreciate everyone's input. I may hold strong opinions but good arguments will always change them. When it comes time to put cash down I may very well be back on Linux.

Excellent and well thought out.

I would like to just add my two cents; I have been dual booting Windows and Linux for about 15 years. 

The reason: I just love computers and software.

I have seen an unbelievable number of changes since my first Linux installation, which by the way took about a month! 
since I didn't know a thing about Unix or networking and there was not really any "support" or "binaries". 

If you wanted to install something, you compiled the source code and possibly a new kernel.
If that didn't work... well it was strait to usenet or a trip to the book store. 

I use, almost exclusively GNU/Linux, but the computers that my wife uses are Windows machines; Win200 and Vista. 
It don't bother me a bit that she wants to use Windows. 
However I do spend hours on those machines fighting spyware, virus and registry problems.

From my perspective, I tinker with my Linux systems because I want to, I tinker with my windows systems so that they will be useable.

An interesting trend has taken place since GNU/Linux has become a viable solution.
You see more Free and Open source software for Windows and as for Linux, its become so easy to install and use
that I truly believe it will become the system of choice for many more people and business driven simply from cost.

Believe it or not, I haven't tried a large number of distros in my 15 year partnership with Linux.
Slackware was my first, next was Redhat then Debian and now Ubuntu, and I can say that the Ubuntu folks 
have done an excellent job making GNU/Linux easy.

There is nothing wrong with using Windows if that is what you need, but keep an open mind.

### Post by leve4919 on 2008-06-30
That's it! 

Today I removed this virus you call operating system ubuntu from the shiny surface of my hard drive.I was rather patient. The system was deteriorating over time with ought my help. I will repeat the main problems I had and hope that this will be somehow communicated to developers.

1. With no "declaration of war" and with no actions on my side (but an automatic update), it stopped automatically connecting to the wireless network at boot time. I checked all relevant files/places (and google). All is absolutely kosher, but nothing helped. So I have to connect manually by pretending that I modified the wireless settings and only then it connects.

2. I was very disappointed when I found, that just received payment transaction ID which I copied to the clipboard (with an intention to paste it into a document), was gone when I closed the browser. Google explained, that the clipboard is crippled by design. To fix the problem I installed "clipboard-daemon" only to find that it:
a. works in 50% cases
b. does not cover all applications (do not want to go into details. I filed a bug).
Somebody suggested to install "Klipper". _Ironically enough it works._
3. _Evolution (the stock "Outlook replacement") silently crashed when I was changing some options._ When I restarted it, half of the standard folders were non accessible (the best was "trash"). To fix it I had to remove all e-mail accounts, delete the "leftover messages" and add accounts again. This time all was almost fine, but I could not synchronize local and server folders.

4. All the sudden,_ "Trash" (not Evolution's, but the "Recycle bin") stopped working. _I did not have rights to purge some files. Google helped me to remove these files in "sudo" mode, and also almost to remove the rest of the file system (almost made this mistake).

5. vi - works. Do not know if this is good or bad.

6. Installed Kdeveloper and could not compile, just created project. I do not have rights to write file into a subdir on my home directory (debug).

7. Still the system is fast.

8. _Still think that cmd's name completion is better then bash's (at least the way I use command line). _

9. Bluetooth keyboard works under windows, but ubuntu can't connect to, giving some preposterous message like use another viewer. _Google tells that this bug is on and off since April and no solution yet._

10. [COLOR="Red"]THE LAST DROP: [/COLOR]after another update reboot was requested. After reboot this piece of junk [COLOR="red"]failed to START HAL.[/COLOR] 

11. Hiren CD is great. In less then hour I'm back in Windows.

12. I like the conclusion of one of the posts: "Let's all agree that linux is just better". :lolflag: Let's agree :) Good luck and see you in 10 years.:KS

### Post by kessaris on 2008-06-30
We really should not get evangelical when it comes to our operating systems, and most importantly we should not try to impose our decisions on others.

To be perfectly honest, there is really no ideal operating system that suits everyone, and in many situations there is no ideal operating system that suits any person at any given time!

I like certain features of certain software.  To list a few:

I like the directory structure of unix.  I dig that all the devices are accessible in a single tree structure.  I love the proc and dev directories.  I like dot files and the command line.

I like the Exposé feature of OSX and the cool little shortcut keys like command-~ that lets you switch through windows of the active application.  I also appreciate how easy it is to type special characters.

In Windows I like the fax and photo viewer, how it lets you create a slide show out of the current folder with very little memory overhead.

Specifically for distributions like ubuntu I like the community support and the package managers that take care of the dependencies of the software I'm installing.

In desktop managers like KDE and gnome, I really like the focus follows cursor behaviour.  That's probably the number one reason I choose linux.

There are also things I don't like about operating systems.

On Linux I get peeved at having to diagnose every last piece of hardware for compatibility.

On OSX I get peeved at the Dock so I use a third party utility called Overflow. Don't get me started about stacks.

Windows gets my goat the most, however.  I hate having to run in power user mode or administrator mode.  Antivirus, anti spyware, windows defender, genuine advantage.. I spend most of my time clicking away annoying **** rather than just having my way with the computer.

I despise having all my hard drives segmented in the my computer view.  Would it kill someone to implement a directory structure where all the drives are part of the same file system? 

Dos?  Screw DOS.  I install Cygwin whenever I need to use the command line.

So you can see.  Every operating system has its ups and downs.  My broad general choice in OSX when I can get it, because it's polished, it's got commercial support.  On PC hardware I use linux as a matter of principle because the downsides of windows to me are far worse.  I just feel better with Linux.  So what if my card reader doesn't work?  

I can't help thinking that if Windows were out of the picture, most video games would be based on OpenGL as they should be.

This is a battle for standards.  Are we going to sit back while external interests tell us what we should be using, or create our standards ourselves?

We are choosing the Unix paradigm because it is a powerful computing framework rather than a closed proprietary system with dubious support that is not in our best interest.

This battle is not about one type of widget over another, it's about open thinking versus proprietary software that we have no control over.

This reminds me of Aesop's fable about the kept dog and the wild dog.

The wild dog came upon a kept dog tied to his dog house and they got to talking.  The kept dog said to the wild dog 'look at you, so shabby and hungry.  I have all the food and water I need and my master cares for me'.  

The wild dog replied: 'yes, you have food and water and comfort, while I am shabby and tired and hungry.  But I am free to go as I please, and you are here, stuck to this post.

This is the battle.  Open versus kept.  I think there is not much more else to say.

---

### Post by kessaris on 2008-07-02
> **gatorbrit said:**
> All I want is to be able to edit stupid equations....


That sounds kind of crazy to me, coming from the University of Toronto.

In my faculty at least, almost every single handout we received and document we ever submitted was created using LaTeX.  More specifically, from early on we were using [the excellent typesetting program LyX]("ttp://www.lyx.org/"), with which you may be familiar.  If I remember correctly this was prior to the introduction of Open Office, but at any rate, this is an entirely different paradigm.  It was only once some of my peers started diverging to wanting to work for Microsoft that tacky Word documents began to surface.

My professors were definitely submitting their papers to journals in the same fashion.  You can even get a sense that the University of Toronto has a standard looking style which is like a default looking LaTeX document.

From their site: > LyX is a document processor that encourages an approach to writing based on the structure of your documents (WYSIWYM), and not simply their appearance (WYSIWYG).

I know that Journals are very friendly to the pdf format, and this is exactly what LyX outputs.  Nothing is ever garbled, the data is stored in a structural form based on what is represents, rather than what it's been formatted to look like.

I really can't stress the greatness of this software enough.  Load up the résumé module and you're typing a résumé.  It supports even documents like screenplays.  The math and science features are innumerable, from equations to matrices, footnotes, figures.

That said, it may sound crazy but it's not surprising.  Here is a forum of people what find themselves in the position of having to convince people of the obvious, facing only resistance.

on behalf of Ubuntuers all over the world, I wish you all the best dear sir!

---

### Post by odinbaal on 2008-07-09
The first thing a friend of mine commented when he saw me using Linux was "Wow! It's SOO quiet, how come?" As a Windows XP and now Vista user, he was used to the unending sound of fans madly whirling and hard disks continuously crunching data.

His second comment, as he got closer, was how quick things start up and, funnily, how quickly they close. I opened Claws-Mail in front of him and it took exactly one second to open, although I have more than a thousand messages in my inbox. And one second to close, with no whirring and hd activity and hourglasses spinning. Try to do this in Windows!

Plus the minimal memory usage. When I start my Linux box, the system is using exactly 66Mb of memory. Can you have this in Windows after installing firewalls and AVs and so on? In Linux, I'm all set, all protected, and ready to go exactly 3 seconds after my desktop shows.

Finally, the peace of mind: as another poster rightly said, Windows is a constant battlefield. When I was on Windows, I was spending my time updating my AV, answering endless questions from my firewall (who never seemed to learn, whatever type it was, and I used many), checking my two anti-malware, and living in a state of siege. In Linux, things are quiet, almost subdued, quick, and reliable.

One thing I learned is that errors in XP are few, but when they happen they tend to be fatal (i.e., full formatting and re-installing); errors in Linux are more numerous, but easily fixed. I prefer the latter option.

---

### Post by Barsaloux on 2008-07-09
I've been a user of both linux and windows for some time (obviously I've been using windows longer since I wasn't aware of Linux for many years), and at the moment i dual-boot XP and ubuntu

First I'd like to say that I've tried Vista, and in pretty much every way possible it is inferior to XP. From what I've experienced it has been very slow at even the simplest tasks, and booting it up seemed to take forever. There have been tests done that even proved that Vista still ran 40% slower than XP at most business tasks with most-all performance hindering processes turned off.

In terms of XP, I have never really had any major problems, but that is not the reason I have kept it. If there is anything I can do on Linux, you can bet that is where I will do it. But that is the thing, that a lot of the things Linux can't do are things I use on a daily basis. A lot of this also stems from the fact that I'm still fond of video games, and have been trying to run Crysis on high/very high settings through consistent upgrading.

While people have also mentioned that the alternatives to most windows programs do to stifle productivity, I have still found more thorough and expert options on XP. A large amount of freeware can compete or sometimes beat the commercial products that do the same thing. That said, I do have Adobe CS3, that I got (MUCH) cheaper from a friend at Best Buy. I have gotten interested in editing photos and movie production, and Adobe is pretty much unparalleled at it's job.

That said, besides those, Linux has Windows beaten. The prospect of never getting viruses makes everything so much easier, but on the same note I never got a virus on XP, but thats due to the heavy protection I kept on constantly. On my main PC it's hardly noticeable, since I have a fairly high end gaming PC, but with my older laptops it has shown its merit: It runs faster and uses less system resources. This can be huge for people without good financial resources. The list of things Linux does better than windows IMO trounces the list of things windows does better than linux.


Those are just my rough thoughts after having used linux and windows extensively, just feedback to let you know my experience and preference because of it.

### Post by Markissocoollike on 2008-07-22
> **helloyo said:**
> I’m writing this thread to show how someone like you moved from being exclusively Linux for years to being exclusively Microsoft. This isn’t because I love Microsoft, or hate Linux, but because I assessed my option and chose which one suited me most. 

A quick bit of history: I started using Linux with Red Hat 9 and was exclusively Linux not long after. Since then I have tried every major distribution and some of the smaller ones. Where I remained for the last couple years was on Ubuntu due to its large user base, professional releases and support. 

Recently I realised I was being too fundamentalist about many things in my life. I was very much set in my ways and was not open to different options. I discovered that I was doing this and made a lot of changes. Then I realised that I hadn’t given Windows a chance in many years. Linux was great and had been a lot of fun, but I wanted to spend more time using my computer than tinkering with it. 

So I took the plunge, wiped Linux and installed my old copy of Windows XP for a trial. Were there issues? Absolutely, but as a Linux user they were no problem to solve. Not only that, but learning the quirks wouldn’t benefit me for one 6 month release cycle, but for several years and on countless computers. I had become clueless about the vast majority of computer systems out there and needed to relearn a lot of it. 

So after years I played around on the seven year old Windows XP. I installed Windows versions of Firefox, Skype, Flash and Opera. They all worked better. Firefox supported more plugins, Skype was a generation ahead, Flash didn’t have Z-Index issues and Opera didn’t have a mismatched menu bar. I then installed software (trials) that I hadn’t used in years: Office 2007, Photoshop CS3 and some games. Their capabilities were phenomenal. Of course they’re not free and cost a fortune to produce, but that’s the point. They are a better product for a higher price. 

I soon took the plunge and bought Vista. It sure isn’t perfect, but it is still fantastic due to what it enables me to do. I can still support free software, but when it is advantageous for me to do it. I use Firefox because it is better, not because it is open source. That is also why I use Office 2007 over OpenOffice.org. 

 I love computers and marvelling at the new things that software can do. I used to think that the best way to pursue this hobby was with Linux but now I’m not so sure. Just playing around with the free, integrated voice recognition in Vista demonstrates that.

Linux is a massive testament to collaboration and what it can achieve. All I’m saying is keep your options open. Sure it costs more money, but considering how much time some of us spend on our computers it may be worth the investment.

...you didn't even give ubuntu a chance? Shame on you! You'll be back thats for sure!

---

### Post by nevdelap on 2008-07-22
After being a software developer for 15 years I switched from being exclusively Microsoft to 'almost' exclusively Linux about two months ago. Every couple of years for about 12 years I had installed Linux to see where it was up to, and never got the urge to switch.

But Windows Vista on my laptop with 1GB of memory ran so abysmally that I couldn't stand it - running a browser and media player, and a couple of other things and having Windows occasionally die in the butt until I figured out which process was using all the memory and killed it was just unacceptable - I'm not going to buy more memory to get around this pathetic performance.

A Linux evangelist happened to do his sales pitch to me around the same time. (Cheers Nelson! - I'm glad you convinced me).

So now I run Ubuntu and I am loving it. I also run Windows XP in a VirtualBox with it's brilliant seamless windows - and everything is running superbly in 1GB.

Not everything is perfect - I can't get my webcam to work, and Linux Skype is flaky compared to the Windows version. But all the positives far outweigh these small problems.

I run Windows because I have and love Babylon, and so that I can run Dreamweaver. I give the virtual machine 320MB, and with everything I regularly use and the virtual machine running Linux is showing only 700MB in use.

I worked with Windows and some Unix for a lot of years, and it took a lot of years for Linux to get to where I thought it was ungeeky enough for me to want to use it - which Ubuntu seems to have made it - so I can't see myself ever going back.

:)

### Post by bilijoe on 2008-07-22
I don't understand why so many people complain about spending more time working on their [Linux] system than using the computer.  I have Linux on 4 computers (7.10 and 8.04.1).  One is a high-end DELL, one is an ooold Gateway, one is a 4 year old Compaq Presario, and one is a "God knows what" that my son bought as a box of parts from someone who was either trying to build a computer from scratch, or had dismantled one, and couldn't get it back together.  It has an obscure MB, a high-end-looking sound card, with no brand name in it I could find, and mis-matched memory modules.  The processor is a "Genuine Intel" 2 GHz Core Duo, and the CD/DVD drive is a SONY (which is the only visible name on the thing, so it LOOKS like it's a Sony, but it's not).  (I included that lengthy description, just to highlight the fact that I am not working entirely with "compatible" hardware here).  

Now, my point:  I haven't spent ANY time working on ANY of these systems (except for installing software, plug-ins, updates, and the like--which doesn't count as "working on the system", in my book).  All four computers are on a LAN, two of them are wireless.  The printer is a network printer, my son's machine (the "box of parts" computer) is loaded down with tons of software, I have no idea what most of it does, and he downloads almost anything that has a button that says "Free Download, click here".  He's even "installed" some Windows weirdos (don't ask me how)--not with WINE or CrossOver, and they don't run, but most did "install" files and icons--and we can't figure out how to get them off the system, so they're just there, you'd think fowling things up.  But this is Linux, so it just tolerates them and keeps on chugging along, handling hours of downloads a day, and lots of hacking around by someone who doesn't really know what he's doing.  You'd think that, at least THAT machine would be giving me trouble.  But, no, all four machines just purr along, with none of them requiring any attention whatever.  

The ONLY thing I do with my Ubuntu Linux machines is use them--to do what computers do.  I've never had to "work on" any of them.  So, I'm curious; what is it that all you people who "work on their machines more than they use them" do?  What kind of work do your systems require?  Are you doing something unusual with them?  I'd just like to hear some details, instead of just grousing about how much work it takes to keep a Linux system up and running.  As I said, I don't count updates, and installing software, or plug-ins, etc., that's simple maintenance, and doesn't prevent me from using the machine for its intended purpose.  

Throw me a post or two, to let me know what all this work is that's necessary to keep your Linux systems running.  I'd like to at least be able to commiserate with you folks, when I read some plaintive post about all work, and no play--because it's a Linux system.

---

### Post by bilijoe on 2008-07-22
> **BEdmonson85 said:**
> the guy has a very valid point...  XP generally works better until you fine tune Linux to your machine.  To some... that process of fine tuning is not worth the hassle. Fine tune??? I guess if you really feel the need, but on all four of my scraggly collection of computers, I just install, download and install the updates, install a little software (most everything I need comes "out of the box"), and a few widgets I like, all of which are just a few mouse clicks away, through Synaptic, or a (oh my God, this is HORRIBLE, a Console Command Line command) "sudo apt-get install blabla" away.  If this is what you mean by fine tuning, then I think it's trivial, and doesn't deserve the time it takes to complain about it.  Pretty much, I run the install, and the [always present] set of updates to catch up with what's been improved and fixed since I burned my install CD, and start using the thing--right "out of the box".  Sometimes I think some of you users expect too much.  Just because Linux is better than Windows, doesn't mean that a Magic Genie is going to pop out and do all the little, menial things it takes to set up ANY computer system.  It's not Magic, it's just a better operating system.  Nuf said?
