### Post by spaceboy909 on 2008-07-23
I've rewritten this post about 5 times trying to shorten it down.....

All perspectives are valid, except those that deny the perspectives of others.

All of these user experiences are valid, and have a serious impact on the person's life, whether negative or positive.

Computers, are machines, and **we build machines to make our lives easier.**

Machines, being technological, are meant to be improved.  Technology and stagnation are two terms that should never be used in the same sentence.  Computers should always be pushing the envelope, in every way, otherwise, we are allowing some form of dogma to override its' natural progression, to serve some ulterior motive.

That's not to say that  an individual user can't choose his own niche, and that niche being one of "doing things the old fashioned way", so to speak, but this choice should never, ever interfere with the progress of technology for the rest of us.  

Neither money nor philosophy should be allowed to hold technology back, **but yet we are allowing them both to do just that!**

Inspite of this, the GPL allows for incredible freedom.  It's coming way too slowly, but nonetheless, the Linux desktop, in perhaps 5 or 10 years, will finally match and surpass Windows in user friendliness.  At that point in time, so many of the arguments that, "*Windows users need to learn more about how the computer actually works (**...no they don't...**)*", will be a moot point, as GNU/Linux itself will have finally met the users on their own turf.

Now, that's about as short as it gets for me.......ever..... heheh.

### Post by mptpro on 2008-07-24
As the first poster said, "I wanted to spend more time using my computer than tinkering with it."

I have been using Ubuntu for almost a year.  I really like it, especially the open-source software.

I was dual-booting with WinXP and later, Vista.  However, when objectively looking at my usage I realized I spent more time in Windows.  Not by choice, but by necessity.

So, on my laptop, at least, I am switching exclusviely to Windows.  My home media-server is still running Ubuntu.

THe primary reasons are some key software and hardware doesn't run on Ubunut, doesn't run well, or is very time consuming to get to work.

For example,

Adobe Acrobat Professional.  I edit/create a lot of pdfs, and this is cumbersome, even impossible, in U.

My Garmin Zumo 550 GPS device doesn't sync with U.

My Blackberry Curve pda/phone doesn't sync with U.

My Logitech 890 univerals remote doesn't sync with U.

Mozy Online Backup service doesn't sync with U.

XetusOne, a mortgage program, doesn't work in U.  Interestingly, this is a online service, but to access it I need IE.

The final straw was a Novatel Merlin X950D ExpressCard for connecting to the Internet via cell service.  Though there are drivers for linux, after spending four hours last night, with no success, I gave up.  My time is just not worth it.  In Windows I plug it in, and works immediately.

I hope to revisit Ubuntu in the future, but I am not married to a method, but an outcome.  That outcome is productivity.

### Post by Tripp_mongo on 2008-07-25
Be sure to close all running applications including your anti-virus when you install/update your various programs one at a time so that you do not have dynamic link library (dll) problems with MS-Windows.  Be sure that you do not make a mistake when you type a web address so as not to import any viruses/spyware, as they are usually written using MS-DOS commands,  into your temporary internet files.

I suggest that you do a dual-boot.  You can easily change grub to boot to Windows by default.   Six months from now when you find your Windows system freezing while the hard drive and memory work 100%, and you become frustrated, then you will have a smoothly working system with Linux.

Honestly, I love people like you.  You are the reason why Windows is so widely used and a major reason why malicious software is written using MS-DOS commands rather than Unix commands.  I do not suggest that you do anything that requires security using MS-Windows, and I hope that you do not encounter any charges from China on your bank accounts or credit cards.

---

1.5 Terrabytes SATA2 HDs

Anyway, what I'm experiencing just after the first install was the unimaginable slow response I get in Hardy in allmost all circumstances of usage. Compiz is turned completely off and its still lagging far behind my expected tempo of use. The thing I value most by operatingsystems are true responsiveness. Which mean that when I scroll, type, move windows, go through menus etc, this should happen as quickly as I can do it myself. Especially when there is really nothing else happening inside my computer that makes it busy. Like just now: I'm sitting two meters from my monitor, so I just wanted to scale up the fonts in FF, but this just took more than 10 seconds and my FF turned dark in the meantime. 

This happens all the way and it's no reason whatsoever that this should occur. I have installed latest Nvidia drivers, have done a clean re-install and really updated my distro on all repositories possible. But I will underline that this was my first impression from the start too, before any additions where added, so I have by no means f***ed up my system by installing third party **** that is regarded as risky. Like now when I writing I write faster than Hardy is able to show me this in realtime. So I just want to say. F**k this ****. I will resist go back to Gutsy, since I think its ******** from Ubuntu developers to give out such **** since they absolutely must have been aware of this before they released it. 

It's just a sad story. Luckily I didn't wipe off XP yet, so I still have a somewhat responsive system. 

I mean: on my calendar it says july 2008. 15 years ago I had a super responsive system (a.k.a AMIGA 1200). It's sad to see how technology has stagnated into such a mess that we see today. 

I like many of the things Hardy Heron has to offer, but it was just too annoying to sit wait for a simple thing as typing text to show up on my monitor. This is so damn sad that I have no words for it. 

My CPY is laxing most of the time, and I have never used any of my swap-space. So not come here and tell me something is not seriously wrong. It's simply a BS-system they have realeased, and there is no way I can trust these developers in their hype. 

My machine is by linux-standards a monster. I saw the responsiveness of gutsy, so I know what to expect. Unfortunately I was asked to automatically upgrade to latest version by the updater, so this resulted in a totally corrupt OS which forced me into a re-install. So I downed HH, since I hoped a few things would be better, but it took me no less than 14 CD's and 4 downloads before I had a copy that was fault-free. And that from a 3 months old LG-DVD/CD-Burner that has made beautiful copies of everything else burned so far. So yes, you have a lot to fix Ubuntu-guys and I seriously mean that calling something more than version 1.0 should mean that it would work ten times faster and stable than it does today. Just holding my backspace in to erase a sentence can cause it to continue erasin more than two seconds after I release the backspace-key. 

F**k this sh*t!! Seriously. I have seen a lot of complaints about this, and everytime I get some updates I hope to see real improvements, but no, it's the same bad thing I get from a fresh reset. Also issues about unexpected crashes and freezing up is a normal thing here. So get your heds together and find out how the hell a machine like mine is less responsive than a Commodore64.

Goood luck and good bye to this annoying crap!!!
I did love it, but they seem to make my decision very easy by distributing an OS like this and letting me sit will all the problems. It's simply not fun anymore. Sadly but true. I hate MS-everything, but sad to see how we are still forced to use this. 
I am not able to find any suggestions on this forum on how to fix this, and yes call me stupid, but I'm not able to find the hidden button that lets me start my own thread on this subject. 

Regards,
Gunnar.

### Post by beow on 2008-07-27
I use linux exclusively at home and almost exclusively at work (since the "dapper" release). To be honest there is a higher level of hazzle with ubuntu to get it working alright in the corporate environment. Sometimes I miss the times of just using the windows OS and go with the flow as dictated by the IT department. No problems (and at least if there is a problem it's not your fault...). I do use XP in a Virtualbox for some must have applications (Visio, some DB front-ends, full Office compatibility when needed). So I have the extra hazzle of choosing which application to use in many cases (ooo or word/XP). Moving files from the virtualbox to ubuntu and vice versa.

The question is: Why do I expose myself to this hazzle? Why don't I follow the path of least resistance?

The answer: I feel good about using linux (and ubuntu in particular). I feel a satisfaction when my Ubuntu is trimmed and running smooth that I never felt with windows. It looks better... I feel proud of using it and be part of the open source movement. In short my inclination to ubuntu is very much more based on irrational reasons than on facts about program quality, sw prices etc. I think this is a psychologic characteristic that is common to "early adopters".

BTW I'm using the XP/Vbox much less than I thought i would...

### Post by bilijoe on 2008-07-27
>                      Originally Posted by **emshains**                     [[IMG]http://ubuntuforums.org/images/buttons/viewpost.gif[/IMG]]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=5470619#post5470619")                 
                 *You left linux because you were lazy, you wanted to use it with ease, but we here are very disciplinary, we say: "Do youre homework(debug the code, fix the problem) and then use it with ease!", or something like that. *
> **jdrodrig said:**
> I must disagree; statements like that are not going to attract more users to linux (if that is your goal). Are you saying, people should expect to work more to do a similar task in linux than in windows?..isn't ubuntu motto, "it just works"? I agree.  Posts like this are of no value.  They are not informative, and don't make a point.  They only serve to irritate or inflame others, and do not reflect well on the Ubuntu Community.  

I came to Linux, because it is the better operating system.  I stayed, and will stay for as long as I am able to use a computer, because of the Community.  

The Ubuntu Community is the Crown Jewel of the Linux World.  Because of that community, most clearly reflected in these forums, I have no interest in other distros.  As far as I'm concerned, Ubuntu IS Linux.  

Nothing any other distro could possibly offer could have enough value to compensate for the fact that Ubuntu, for me, is more than an operating system--much more.  It is a way of life, a philosophy.  In fact, I want to say that Ubuntu is not an operating system at all.  It is a community of like-minded individuals, who communicate and cooperate, and share a collective project that, as a side benefit of belonging, provides its members access to one of the best distros of one of the best operating systems in the world, Ubuntu-Linux.  

I came for the OS.  I stayed, for the Community.
______________________________________

I think there has been a certain amount of stress and tension created recently, by the fact that the world of home computing is currently in flux.  I don't know if statistics support it, but I see a mass migration underway, which is causing a great influx of users to the Linux world.  Most stay, some don't, but without our having any control over it, the Linux world is growing at a record pace.  

As a result, we are experiencing "growing pains".  As people change, allegiances change.  Some become passionate about their discovery of a new world, and earnestly try to convince others to change their allegiances--to adopt this new and better way.  Others, passionately defend that which they have known and trusted for so long and, as is the way for some, resist change, and strongly advocate that others do the same.  

We all must live in the same world, like it or not.  And we all have an inherent right to choose for ourselves, AND*** to say what we think***, and support what we believe in.  Rudeness and personal attacks, serve no purpose here.  They only sap energy from more honorable pursuits, and sully the landscape.  

This is not a soccer game.  It is an intellectual pursuit; an exploration of new territory, and a quest to discover all it has to offer, and to push the boundaries to new, exciting and rewarding limits.  Let's all sit back for a minute, take a deep breath, and remind ourselves what it is that we are really doing.  

To those few who find that what they are really doing, is looking for a place to vent their frustration over the complexities of the modern world, I would ask, please go somewhere else.  To the rest I would say, please, let's [re]turn our energies to the real goal of making this world a better place for all of us, by arguing our points and articulating our differences in a civil and respectful manner.

[SIZE=2]**"I disapprove of what you say, but I will defend to the death your right to say it."**[/SIZE]
_[[COLOR=Blue]Evelyn Beatrice Hall[/COLOR]]("http://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Evelyn_Beatrice_Hall")_ (1868 - 1919)

---

> **phaed said:**

How many people really need those advanced features?  Not your average user, that's for sure. In three years of exclusively using Linux, there's nothing that OpenOffice.org and The GIMP couldn't do for me.

Yes, Firefox is more buggy on Linux, but I'm not about to pay $300 to get a Windows version of it.  It's a simple cost-benefit analysis.  Ubuntu/Linux does 99% of what I want, and I'm not going to pay $300 for that extra 1%.
Office07 and Photoshop weren't made for average users. No person is going to pay the hefty price-tag for Photoshop, they'll use GIMP or Photoshop-Online. Normal people buy Office 07, but it was indeed created for the business market to meet an array of niches.

---

### Post by LibertyShadow on 2008-07-28
> **adldesigner said:**
> <-- 27 years old married user who loves to go out on weekends, work during the week and generally be more productive with his computer.

Uses Ubuntu. Won't go back. Too much productivity gained. Unquestionable bang for your buck. And finally, loves the whole I-ve-got-an-issue-sudo-something-done-with-the-issue.


YES!! Finally, someone who understands me!  My first issue was my Dell Inspiron E1505 wireless (broadcom... :( ).   How did I solve my problem?  Check out the first result on a google search of "E1505 Wireless": [http://www.google.com/search?hl=en&q=E1505+wireless+&btnG=Google+Search]("http://www.google.com/search?hl=en&q=E1505+wireless+&btnG=Google+Search") 

I recently helped two of my housemates dualboot who were growing weary of Vista's... well, Vista.  They are serious pros at copying and pasting commands to install dvd playback, add repositories to their sources, etc... So far they love it.  

Personally, I have had my fair share of issues with Ubuntu, particularly related to the hard drive (heat, power management, ata error messages etc...)  but they usually are issues that I have caused myself through messy tweaking and un-tweaking.  

If someone were to ask me what the number one feature is in Ubuntu, I would have to talk about the community.  Need I say more after bilijoe's post?  

Windows doesn't always just work.  Ever try installing Microsoft Zune drivers and software on Windows XP Pro x64 Edition?  Funny that the only incompatibility I ran into with XP Pro x64 was Microsoft's fault.

Also, I found myself relying on so many third party programs that it was difficult to always be running the latest version of each program.   Not to mention virus/spyware definitions!! Sometimes I click on the Windows security notifications when they say, "Click here to solve this issue,"  just so I can laugh when it is impossible to solve the issue from there.
In comparison, the repository system is brilliant and easy.

As long as there is: 
a) A community
b) Excellent 64bit functionality
c) A maintained repository of open-source solutions
Ubuntu will be my OS of choice.

### Post by michael1234 on 2008-07-28
Linux n00b here. Here´s why, unlike the OP, I won´t be leaving Linux:

In late June our power supply for our desktop blew out, and took the NVIDIA card with it. So now we had to get a new computer quick (my wife uses Second Life, which appears to be available on Linux and OS X, but she didn´t want to take any chances). Besides, the [COLOR="DarkRed"]computer uses an AGP slot[/COLOR] :( we had to move on.

We got a 64-bit Vista system (wanted XP, but it was already July and all the XP machines were out by June 30 :mad: . Nice and powerful, but won´t shut down properly and there are all kinds of little fixes you have to apply to get Flash to work, etc. We´ve only had it for a month or so, haven´t installed too much; so far, the biggest problem happened when our antivirus stopped updating when we, er, did something (my wife was freaking out that she hadn´t installed anything around the time it stopped). So we just reinstalled the a/v from the mfg site and re-entered the key for updates. No biggie I have a basic tolerance for necessary tinkering as long as it is not an hours/days-long process.

We still have our old desktop, sans NVIDIA card (works fine for normal stuff with the onboard chipset).

[COLOR="Indigo"]The kicker: we have a laptop from our school days when we would take it to school and take notes and post them on a blog for the whole class to read ;-)

This laptop is getting rather old, and the XP had slowed to a crawl. So I decided once we got the new machine to try Ubuntu on the laptop. We both have musical experience and drive, but the hankering to try ProTools on a MacBook Pro is killed by cost. Enter Ubuntu Studio.[/COLOR]

At the moment, I don´t need to do any high-level professional work. I have used ProTools and I created a bunch of awesome tunes on a standalone Ensoniq ASR-10 sequencer/sampler synthesizer. But I really need a GUI, especially when tweaking wave files or tracks. The 1990s-spec hardware I have can´t do any longer.

I wanted to set up a dual-boot but I [COLOR="DarkRed"]SCREWED UP THE PARTITION![/COLOR] Windows XP is gone from the laptop. Not what I had in mind, but now I am no longer wading in the Ubuntu pond. I have to swim!

My impressions (I comment only on what I have experience using on both OSs):

[COLOR="DarkGreen"]*Ubuntu runs much faster and more consistently than Windows XP. In the middle of the summer, I can run Firefox with 10 tabs open without overheating the 1.6 Ghz processor. Awesome.

*Browsing is faster in both Firefox and Opera. One big difference: [COLOR="DarkRed"]no matter how many tabs you have open, pages still load at the same speed[/COLOR]. In Windows each new Firefox/Opera tab is a step closer to a grinding halt.

*One more thing about browsing: thanks to [this post]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showpost.php?p=5237383&postcount=20") I am definitely doing all my online payments and sensitive transactions through Ubuntu, and not Windows!

*The GNOME UI is faster generally. The Main Menu, however, responds more slowly than XP´s start menu.

*Networking has been a bit of trouble. When the wireless connects, it´s noticeably faster than it was in XP. However, I can´t get the wireless to pick up automatically when I start up the computer. I have to do this weird manual switching, and then it works! This was never a problem in XP (see [http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=5472892](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=5472892) for more on this)

*DVD burning is much more reliable and easier to control with Brasero than Nero.

*Totem seems to be sleeker than Windows Media Player. It loads both streams and cached content quickly and the preferences have handy user controls to adjust the picture. Never saw that in WinXP, though I admit I never poked around for it.

*Still getting used to the file system. At the moment I am more comfortable with Windows, but this may be due to 14 years of A:/ C:/ D: and /program files and windows/system. Old habits die hard.

*I had a problem with the sound driver in Ubuntu, which was pretty easily fixed. Now the sound is clearer and sharper than it ever was with XP! That´s definitely a plus for me, given that I want to work with audio.

*I have used OpenOffice and GIMP in Windows. Looking forward to using them in Ubuntu.

*Shortcut keys are different, and sometimes inconsistent. CTRL+X/C/V does clipboard stuff in Windows and some Ubuntu apps, but the terminal requires CTRL and SHIFT at the same time, and CTRL+C is quite another thing. In addition, I like being able to use ALT+LEFT ARROW in Firefox on Windows to go back. Can´t do this in Ubuntu Firefox, why?[/COLOR]

It seems all in all like I can do what I need to do on Ubuntu, so I am a convert! It will be put to the test more thoroughly, but so far I like what I see and hear.

I still have Windows XP on the old desktop and at work, as well as Vista on the new machine. I´m far from totally comfortable with Linux, and I´ve got a lot to learn. But I cut my teeth 14 years ago on a Packard Bell computer with Windows 3.11 on it :lolflag:  Plenty of crashing, cussing, hard drive formatting, messing with autoexec.bat, trying out DOS commands with customer reps over the phone, etc. That was the school of hard knocks. If I could get through that, I can navigate Ubuntu´s weak points.

I think what is most exciting about GNU/Linux systems is that [COLOR="SeaGreen"]freedom[/COLOR] people talk about. I can´t wait to build a consistent pendrive system that I can just slip into any machine. I can´t wait to hook up MIDI equipment and suddenly be making awesome music for next to nothing. Heck, I have so many audio applications I didn´t spend a dime on I don´t even know where to begin!

Some people have said that as Linux grows, people will write more viruses for Unix and it´ll be just as much of a battlefield as Windows is today. I partially agree... but there are a few mitigating factors:

[COLOR="Navy"]*Linux comes in many flavors. Virus writers are less likely, even when Linux gets popular, to be able to penetrate systems as quickly/uniformly as with Windows.

*The GNU/open source community is a blessing. Sure, there are problems--but so many people out there come up with solutions. I remember trying StarOffice when it was free--wasn´t even *close* to Word, and nor could it read Word´s files. Now OpenOffice competes with Office ´03--*all through volunteer reverse engineering and know-how!* All those minds at work, hacking and compiling! If a virus attacks, we´ve got some of the best people in the world on our side, with no memory-hogging or $40 renewal fees involved. We´ll stop those trojans before even 5% of the user base knows about them.

*Finally, and most important of all, Linux users are smart. Nothing against Windows users--but the very nature of using Linux makes the user become more in control of things. A Linux user is more likely to be able to spot an inconsistency caused by a bug or virus; a Linux user is usually more confident and better able to self-diagnose inconsistencies and look for and apply answers; a Linux user has to have a basic understanding of how a computer works beyond just ¨power on, click and type.¨ Such better-educated users are less likely to download questionable files, get snagged in phishing scams, make modifications they have no idea about to their system, fool with junk emails, etc.

If anything, UNIX viruses will probably hurt Mac users more, since many of them switched from Windows to avoid having to deal with code/scripts/fixes of any sort.[/COLOR]

I am a firm and proud member of the GNU/Linux/Ubuntu community. Though the road may be rocky at times, I will not run screaming back to Windows if I run into a snag in Ubuntu. Like most users, I will keep Windows available to me, because a multi-pronged approach is good. My experience hacking my old clunky Packard Bell, though it put me through 2 months of hell, taught me enough about Windows that I am comfortable in it, bugs and fixes and all. The Linux learning curve can´t be that sharp, and besides, it´s way more fun and interesting.

Keeping this wonderful thread on topic: as for the OP, indeed you should use Windows if it best suits you. A Toyota Prius is a great car for almost anything, but if you need to haul heavy lumber then a gas-guzzling truck is for you, because it´s also a lumber-hauling truck, and that´s what you need...there´s nothing wrong at all with that. I´ve still got mine when I need it.

Peace out to all my GNU/Linux peeps ´cross the world! Word up!

---

Still, great story Michael1234. Good luck with it all. All's you need is an open mind as well as the open source, and you're away. You've already come armed with that, so you'll be just fine. ;)

### Post by mordak13 on 2008-07-29
Well, I have been using Linux for almost 12 years. Exclusively for over 3. I don't miss Windows one bit. Sadly, there are a couple of pieces of hardware the force me to pull out the spare hard drive for my laptop that has XP on it to only update my Garmin and TomTom GPS'. Other than that I have no other need or desire to use Windows. I find that Linux, Ubuntu, Mint, eLive, and Debian all do what I need with aplomb. I love the fact they are community supported and that I get security fixes as they are discovered and coded not months later because the board decided it wasn't a big enough issue to release a patch for and in the meantime, my system is wide open to exploit for anyone with the know how on how to do so.

Sure Linux and all it's derivative distros aren't perfect but the chances of it being more perfect are far greater with the model they use to make, distribute, and support it. Unlike any other OS available out there - IMHO of course. 

Similarly, the fact that I have 3 kids, a dog, turtle, cat, and two birds, along with a wife, an ex-wife truck and a motorcycle oh yeah and and a healthy mortgage payment, all contribute substantially to my need and desire for Linux. However, after using it as long as I have I find I love the challenge of trying to find an open source alternative to all of the pay software you find for Windows. To me this isn't as much about Windows vs. Linux but about what works for me as an individual consumer. It's also about the principle of the whole hacker mentality which spawned the computer age as we know it. I don't refer to hacker in the "exploit you bank records" type but to the hacker that believes all information should be free and that we should be able to hack anything we own for learning and informational purposes.

I'll keep using Linux until they pry it from my cold dead hands.

### Post by myfigurefemale on 2008-07-29
since i got linux about four months ago, i have had many of the common problems, no wireless, having to re-install because i messed up the bios, spending hours to get one piece of hardware working, etc, etc.  despite that, i STILL have had a much better experience on ubuntu than windows.  first of all my laptop is 3 years old and runs like **** on windows.  ubuntu gave me a much more powerful and useful system where i can do much more.  and now that i have wireless working, i actually have no complaints!  i bought this computer with a 3 year warranty, and straight out of the box the dvd player didn't work - the sound did not catch up with the picture.  i was told it was a "software problem" and to send it in.  not wanting to send my laptop in to HP to never be seen again, i gave up.  with ubuntu, dvds play fine.  when i have a problem, i can go to the forums and have my issue resolved in at most a couple hours.  definitely worth tinkering to not have to send a laptop in or pay to get it repaired when there are issues.  i am an above average user but not an expert in any way, and am mostly figuring things out as i go along.  with windows i was constantly having to mess around to get the computer to do things the way i wanted to do them.  with linux there is no bloatware and everything runs the way it should.  for example, windows media player doesn't pop up every time i put a cd in, completing freezing the computer every damn time.  for me, all of these far outweight the slight pro of everything being windows compatible.  everything is linux compatible too - if you spend the time on it.  and windows is not "plug and play", it's plug and pray that your device is recognized.  never once gotten anything to work in plug and play.  much easier to spend a couple minutes typing sudo apt get whatever than waiting ten minutes while windows tells me ten different ways it's found my new hardware.

### Post by tiachopvutru on 2008-07-29
> **ukripper said:**
> Wireless support is also great on 8.04 or 7.10! ndiswrapper and madwifi made it possible, therefore now about 85% of wireless cards work on linux based OS.

When you are starting out, it's a pain if your wireless device requires ndiswrapper to work, though... mainly because you have yet to learn about ndiswrapper, how to install ndiswrapper, and how to install drivers using ndiswrapper (and to find the driver). Even if ndiswrapper is in the Ubuntu CD, beginners don't know the **sudo apt-cdrom add** command.

When I started out, I used the Xubuntu CD and had to compile ndiswrapper from source (and went through a painful lesson that I needed the **build-essential** package to compile source code). 

And oh, I didn't leave Ubuntu. :)

### Post by stefansjs on 2008-08-07
I think you make some very good points.  Especially when you can get Vista and Office 2007 for cheap with university discounts.  I have the same deal, and right now I am dual booting between 8.04 and Vista.  I was really impressed with how little I had to tweak on Ubuntu to make it work, but I still wouldn't have had to survive if I hadn't spent years learning linux under the guidance of my friend and the Ubuntu Community.  Windows is the same, except there's more windows computers out there.  As for paying for what you get, I think that Office 2007 is leagues ahead of openoffice.org and I would never use openoffice.org for anything remotely official.  You may say that office is just new eye candy, but in some cases that's important - you can create professional looking styles with hardly any effort at all.
My opinion: Ubuntu is great (even better than windows or Mac) for basic day-to-day use, but for productive use, meaning for work/class, I will take windows.

I also just wanted to comment on the free thing: virtually nobody uses computers for free.  If they're windows users they pirate copies of photoshop or other professional software, and if they're linux users they use non-free software to play DVDs and other media.

### Post by decoherence on 2008-10-15
The think the OP makes an excellent point. While I wouldn't hold up Photoshop or any other Adobe (or macromedia) product as a shining example, the big things for me are audio recording, midi sequencing, video editing and animation. In these fields, various Linux offerings pale in comparison to expensive programs like AfterEffects or Cubase. Blender 2.48 is fantastic, but have you seen Softimage XSI 7?? (watch a few demos if you want to be impressed)

Unfortunately, these programs are not accessible to most of us -- at least, not if we want to make any money. I'm not militantly against pirating for personal or educational use, but if you're using the software professionally, I think you should definitely own it. 

So if you want to make your legitimate start, what do you do? You fire up blender and inkscape and the gimp and ardour (or eclipse and vim and/or emacs) and all of these fantastically functional programs that enable you to make beautiful, saleable things *for free*.

Once you're making your money you have the option of 'upgrading' to a commercial program and if you're lucky seeing some kind of productivity gain.

Some people decide to stick around; get involved with the community and try to get more from what they have. These people typically become contributors of some sort, and they improve things for the next generation of users. Rinse. Repeat.

Welcome to the ecosystem!

---

### Post by michaelkahl on 2008-10-15
I'm big into digital photography.  I shoot Nikon equipment currently and used Nikon Capture 4.x for years.  Then I found Bibble Pro.  It's closed source, but at least supports the Open Raw project.  Also, they provide an excellent product that does a great job of editing my camera's raw files in Linux.  I'm able to (with my license) choose between running on a Mac, Linux, or Windows.  I choose Linux because I can't afford a Mac, not sure I want one tbh, and I'm tired of Windows.  People complain about Linux and software not being perfect, but at least support is there most of the time and a fix is on the way.  
After I spent $120.00 on Nikon Capture XP SP2 came out and broke it and neither Nikon or Microsoft cared to address the issue with it.  I had to do my own work around each time I installed Windows or reinstalled the product.  
Thats just an example of how users can be left in the cold, even with "commercial software".  
This isn't to say that Linux is the best for everything, I do think you as the user need to evaluate your needs and take a serious look at what different operating systems offer you.  For some Mac's are the way to go, others want Windows.  I use Linux because **"I"** am able to do everything I need to and enjoy the security and stability I've found in Linux.  XP would always end up giving me fits after time, and Vista is just a resource hog.  
So, to any who choose Windows or Mac I wish you well.  I know you've made that decision for your own benefit, because we all have different computer needs.

### Post by tsali on 2008-11-04
I suppose my story might sound a bit different...

I have used NOTHING but Apple Computers since my first home PC in 1992. In 2001, I built a PC and installed Mandrake.

Mandrake was a real headache to get everything sorted out on, but eventually got there. Still found myself having to deal with incompatible file formats, media and websites, but I always had to deal with that on the Macs as well.

Although I had never owned a Windows based computer, I was determined to avoid all of the horrific experiences I've seen enumerated even in this thread.

Maintaining the Mandrake machine became increasingly problematic. Simple "security updates" were hosing X configuration and undoing hard fought manual configurations. 

I finally had one update too many of this sort.

In October of 2006, I built a new machine to OpenSUSE spec and attempted to install OpenSUSE 10.1 - IT WAS A DISASTER. It was so bad that I just go tin the car, drove to Office Depot and bought Windows XP Home retail.

My install experience with WinXP was fast and flawless. Contrary to what I'd come to expect from my previous Mac and Linux experience, EVERYTHING WORK PERFECTLY!!! I had allowed the Windows installer to connect to the internet and retrieve all of the hardware drivers automatically. It WORKED OOTB.

I was taken aback at how EASY this was.

So now I was prepared for the inevitable reliability issue soon to follow...you know, malware, registry problems, BSODs, slowdowns, reinstalls, et al.

I installed a solid automatic AV program and setup all of the other maintenance tasks to execute automatically.

The system ran smoothly and FAST. It did so perfectly for the next 6 months. I simply could not believe my good fortune!

I then installed Vista Premium. I experienced the same flawless install and excellent post install performance.

Since then, I've setup several computers for friends, family and clients...and I've never seen a BSOD or had to do a reinstall. I've only seen one case of user inflicted malware.

All of that said, I missed using Linux...it was what I knew and we typically prefer what we are most familiar with. 

I installed Mepis on a second hard disk. It worked quite well, but never really measured up to the performance and reliability of Vista on the other drive. 

I then installed Ubuntu 7.10 and thoroughly enjoyed using it...but again, it was plagued with hardware compatibility problems (remember that this system was built out of the SUSE HCL!). As a result, I found myself rarely booting Ubuntu.

I was surprised to find it no fast than Vista...I had expected it to be faster but it wasn't.

I upgraded to 8.04 hoping to resolve the hardware troubles only to see them get worse. 8.10 continues that downward trend.

All the while, the Vista install continues to soldier along without the first hint of issue.

I would LOVE to be able to go back to my roots and retire Windows altogether, but I've become accustom to my Vista install's flawless reliability and availability. I do not tinker with it...I simply use it.

Until I can be reasonably assured of a similar experience with Ubuntu (or other distro), linux remains relegated to second hard drive status.

---

### Post by helloyo on 2008-11-05
I thought now would be a good time to write a follow up to my previous posts. I have been using Vista and XP throughout this time, with some of my work being done on Ubuntu and Kubuntu systems. 

I have still been keeping up with the Linux world, because it is far more interesting than the Windows world. Despite this I have no interest using it as my main operating system anymore. As far as I'm concerned the two main tasks of an operating system are running hardware and running software. 

With Vista ALL of my hardware runs as the manufacturer intended. My monitor is recognised, my motherboard boosts the mic port, my multimedia keys just work, my graphics and TV cards work flawlessly. Not to mention obscure graphics tablets and mobile phone connectivity. Yes it can all be done on Linux but until manufacturers release universally compatible drivers for their products hardware will never work exactly as it should. 

As for software, there is nothing I miss from Linux. The big OSS applications are available for Windows but the same can't be said in reverse. Once again, this isn't a fault of Linux, but an advantage of Microsoft's userbase. 

I am still interested in Linux as a testament to collaboration and as a source of entertainment, but so long as Windows holds market share it has a huge advantage. Hardware and software are made to work with it. Yes this disadvantages Linux but is advantageous to me so long as I use Windows, and I can't find a reason why I shouldn't.

---

### Post by zaivala on 2008-11-09
itayg, that's a silly reason to stop using Ubuntu... when you can leave it on as a Dual Boot.  Using Wubi, you could install Ubuntu and only use up 15 Gb of your hard drive -- and if you're using the graphics software you mentioned, you probably have a huge hard drive that would hardly miss a measly 15 Gbs.

I am a professional editor.  Due to errors still existing in OpenOffice.org, I must use Mickeysoft Word.  Also, my printer is not very Linux-friendly, so I do all the printing I need to do while I'm in Windoze.  Then I reboot to Ubuntu and get that friendly, at-home feeling for all the rest of my computing needs.

I've also had some problems over the years that have caused me to have to reinstall Windoze several times, losing a lot of not-backed-up data, and have solved that by keeping all my personal files on an external hard drive.  It's easy to redirect both Linux and Windoze to look for all my documents on the external drive, so I'm gaining flexibility and reliability, and losing nothing.

### Post by linux4life88 on 2008-12-05
Why I completely removed XP from Laptop and run Ubuntu only:

1) Stability
2) Security
3) Vast amount of High Quality Software (that is also free)
4) Updates (New operating system every 6 months)
5) Doesn't nag (Windows does nothing but wine for you to do this and that, Ubuntu doesn't)
6) Compiz

In short, I prefer to use a product that is community created and supported than a product that is created for control and dictatorship. I don't like companies telling how I can and can't use my software. It's on my computer and I should be able to use it and edit it in any way shape or form that I want. I also believe that using software is not a privilege but a right.

### Post by Isitawonderfulday on 2008-12-06
Dear Ubuntufolk:

I came back to look at the forum out of nostalgia after an 11 month absence or so.  I was intrigued by this thread, because I had started a similar thread which suffered the fate of being 'canned': it was shunted off to 'frequent discussions' or something--a hole in the wall where no one goes.

It seems to me that my experiences as recorded before are germane to the present thread.  They are recorded at

[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=651067](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=651067)

However, since I have not logged on to the forum in ages, I just didn't know where my password was, so I opened a new account.  I assure you all that I'm not a troll but 'Whatawonderfulday'.

I dropped Ubuntu.  I don't really regret it.  Yesterday I sent off a 200,000 word manuscript to a fellow acting as agent, for submission to publishers.  I did all the preparation using Windows + Word. I didn't have a problem.  I got the job done.  That's what's important. While I used to program, I'm getting older and concentrating more on my professional activities than on computer issues.  I think this is a key point.

But here is what I originally wrote, with an update, and somewhat edited:

[INDENT]Here's what I found in using Linux:

Under Ubuntu Feisty (Gnome), when I had defined multiple desktops the taskbar crashed occasionally on boot, forcing a reboot. I posted a bug, but gave up when I had to install trace software but didn't understand the scripting conventions.

Installing the driver for a pcmcia card modem required communication with several PhD Linux experts.

I could never install FAX services. This required communication with several PhD experts in Linux but the instructions were over my head--and I'm not dense.

I changed to Kubuntu

First of all, on installing KDE and deinstalling Gnome, the multiple keyboard feature did not work and I had to do a clean Kubuntu install.

Still no FAX.

On Kubuntu, on the KDE desktop, the desktop icons wander on boot unless I use a reduced set and they are placed just so.

On Kubutu Kweather doesn't work.

I have not been able to make head or tail of how to install wireless drivers for the Internet for any version of Ubuntu. I went to the guide forum but I couldn't follow the instructions given the two cards I have--either the card wasn't listed or the instructions were beyond my comprehension.

On Kubuntu, occasionally my screen goes blank and I have to reboot--I don't have a keyboard or anything when the screen goes blank. On the forums I noticed that someone had the same problem with Ubuntu and that it probably had something to do with an overheating processor--but I run Linux at low CPU speed to save power and I have never had this problem either with Ubuntu or with Windows: that would suggest to me a software bug in Kubuntu (i.e. KDE).

Neither the Ubuntu nor the Kubuntu Gutsy Live CD works.

I believe that I have posted questions about all of these matters on the forums, and to most of these problems I haven't even received a reply.

For the most part, all of the above problems either are solved virtually automatically in Windows XP Pro (e.g. FAX services, pcmcia modem and wireless drivers) or don't exist (e.g. blank screens, non-functional applets).

I just don't have the level of problem that I have with Linux with Windows XP Pro on a dual boot machine.

[INDENT](Here, since I went back to Windows, I would say that I have found the following issue:

Explorer sometimes crashes when I have been using Hibernate.)[/INDENT]

This is not to say that Windows is perfect, but it is to say that in my experience--much to my surprise--it has a higher level of reliability than Linux AT THE LEVEL OF THE USER INTERFACE.

Linux is interesting to me theoretically as an alternative and it is practically useful in my particular situation because it gives me control over power consumption where XP doesn't--but if my experience with Linux is any indication, shouldn't people who have senior positions in the Linux community give serious thought to the useability and reliability of Linux?

[INDENT](Once I went back to Windows I searchd the Net and found free software that allows me to control power consumption just like in Linux.  Ditto an alternative to Windows Multiple Desktops.  I am happy with these alternatives.)[/INDENT]

Moreover, if we are to escape the Microsoft development model of larger and more bloated operating systems (2000 > XP > Vista), the only alternatives are to go to Linux or to Apple. But with this level of user difficulties with Linux, how can Linux possibly make a serious dent in Windows' market share? And for the privilege of using the aristocrat of computers, the Apple, you pay an aristocratic sum.
[/INDENT]

Here is a second post:

[INDENT]Let me make some further remarks.

First of all, thanks to all of you who replied.

Next, to clarify something, I use a Dell Inspiron 2600 laptop. I am not in a position to rebuild it (!) or to throw it away and buy another one. Yes it's a little old, but it works fine, for the moment at least, and it's been upgraded on the hardware side. Windows XP works fine on it, just like it works anywhere else, and I have done a reinstall now that SP3 has been released. It pains me to admit it to my friends, but at the moment (with Feisty), Windows XP is MORE stable than Linux. Since there is an issue with my even running the Gutsy Live CD (probably the Intel 830 Graphics card), upgrading to Gutsy is a little uncertain, although evidently it can be done from the Internet, as indeed turned out to be the case.

Next, for me things like FAX are 'mission-critical'. There is no possibility whatsoever of my relying solely on Linux if it can't deliver FAX services. But I shouldn't have to have a PhD in computer science to obtain FAX services with Ubuntu.

Next, I really have tried to get solutions. I've gotten tired of the whole procedure: it's pot-luck on the forum who will reply with what level of expertise.

I'm not a computer scientist, although I have programmed, but my career path has been such that I really have no interest in 'nerditude': I need a computer to do certain things which are 'mission-critical', not as a hobby in computer science. Here Linux really has a problem crossing a barrier into general acceptance. Possibly, both Red Hat and Canonical are trying to solve that problem at the level of the paid support systems they are offering (i.e. Enterprise and Canonical paid support), but the unpaid support is really very dicey.

So what I am trying to point out to the 'powers that be'--if anyone is listening--is that to get general market acceptance for Linux, it has to cross the divide from 'nerditude' to a product that does what EVERYONE wants, and out of the box. Yes some computers can run it out of the box. Others... It just can't be that way if you want to achieve general acceptance.

While it is probably 'heretical' to remark this, let me say it: Yes Linux under the hood is probably--certainly, let me say--a better operating system than Windows, especially for, say, 'mission-critical' server applications. Very stable, very sound. But as an end-user product... Part of the problem--and here is the heresy--is the development model of 'Open Source': Everyone volunteers their time in one big happy family. Canonical seems to realize that it can't rest there, because it is providing support to enable Dell to load Ubuntu into some of its models at the factory: obviously that sort of operation needs employees somewhere who will 'punch in' and fix whatever is wrong whenever it comes up.

Now I don't have a solution, I do think that the Community should start to think about the long-term future of Ubuntu, and Linux, and consider these issues, whatever solutions it might want to come up with.
[/INDENT]

Next, what really killed Ubuntu was this: when I did the complete Windows Reinstall, I was obliged to format the c: partition.  But that left open the question what I really wanted to do, since I would have to reinstall Kubuntu and would only be able to do it as a Gutsy upgrade to Feisty.  My HD is only 80 GB and I thought, well I can use the space for large texts, so why don't I just can Ubuntu and go to a single installation?

With best wishes to all, starting with Canonical,

Whatawonderfulday

---

### Post by inobe on 2008-12-07
> **helloyo said:**
> I’m writing this thread to show how someone like you moved from being exclusively Linux for years to being exclusively Microsoft. This isn’t because I love Microsoft, or hate Linux, but because I assessed my option and chose which one suited me most. 

A quick bit of history: I started using Linux with Red Hat 9 and was exclusively Linux not long after. Since then I have tried every major distribution and some of the smaller ones. Where I remained for the last couple years was on Ubuntu due to its large user base, professional releases and support. 

Recently I realised I was being too fundamentalist about many things in my life. I was very much set in my ways and was not open to different options. I discovered that I was doing this and made a lot of changes. Then I realised that I hadn’t given Windows a chance in many years. Linux was great and had been a lot of fun, but I wanted to spend more time using my computer than tinkering with it. 

So I took the plunge, wiped Linux and installed my old copy of Windows XP for a trial. Were there issues? Absolutely, but as a Linux user they were no problem to solve. Not only that, but learning the quirks wouldn’t benefit me for one 6 month release cycle, but for several years and on countless computers. I had become clueless about the vast majority of computer systems out there and needed to relearn a lot of it. 

So after years I played around on the seven year old Windows XP. I installed Windows versions of Firefox, Skype, Flash and Opera. They all worked better. Firefox supported more plugins, Skype was a generation ahead, Flash didn’t have Z-Index issues and Opera didn’t have a mismatched menu bar. I then installed software (trials) that I hadn’t used in years: Office 2007, Photoshop CS3 and some games. Their capabilities were phenomenal. Of course they’re not free and cost a fortune to produce, but that’s the point. They are a better product for a higher price. 

I soon took the plunge and bought Vista. It sure isn’t perfect, but it is still fantastic due to what it enables me to do. I can still support free software, but when it is advantageous for me to do it. I use Firefox because it is better, not because it is open source. That is also why I use Office 2007 over OpenOffice.org. 

 I love computers and marvelling at the new things that software can do. I used to think that the best way to pursue this hobby was with Linux but now I’m not so sure. Just playing around with the free, integrated voice recognition in Vista demonstrates that.

Linux is a massive testament to collaboration and what it can achieve. All I’m saying is keep your options open. Sure it costs more money, but considering how much time some of us spend on our computers it may be worth the investment.

once a linux user' always a linux user......

### Post by d2globalinc on 2008-12-11
We will no longer sell windows as the HOST -OS on any new hardware.  Vista64 doesn't perform nearly as good as Ubuntu64 Hardy.   We install a customized Ubuntu OS for all of our clients then windows apps are now hosted in the datacenter or if necessary to run locally we use Vmware Workstation 6.5 and run windows XP as a virtual machine.  XP and Vista both perform better than they do if they were the HOST OS.  And we can keep them isolated so they can easily be restored to a snapshot if they get a virus, spyware, or adware - which is only usually a matter of time.

I built my latest personal workstation to test the "Future" of the desktop almost a year ago now.  I started with Vista64, then after giving SP1 a good run through we decided to goto ubuntu64 and give it a shot.  Windows will never be installed as the HOST OS on a desktop or server of ours (or our clients) again (hasn't been on one of our servers for years).

Here is the original youtube video demo we did after getting Ubuntu64 Hardy up and running ( [http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1DWzuIreDGA](http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1DWzuIreDGA) ) with the thread on how we setup the multiple screens in hardy here ( [http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=884161](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=884161) ).  Since this video was made we now have all of our apps available including Adobe CS4 (Photoshop, flash, etc, using Vmware workstation 6.5.  So we have the best of both worlds until Adobe begins releasing their software for the virtual environment instead of windows (its only a matter of time before everyone does - there is more $$ in it and hardware is powerful enough to run it at native speeds in a virtual environment - and only getting faster!).  Virtualization, hosted apps, and web applications is allowing us to transitions the desktop PC away from windows and onto open source operating systems like Ubuntu.  Its also a good point to make that it was really Microsofts release of Vista that prompted most of our clients to start looking for alternatives to windows.  Our clients really hated getting those brand new notebook computers that ran slower than their 3 year old notebooks with windows xp on them.  So for that, thanks Vista! ;)  It's my job to be a consultant and look at all the options and then find the best solution that fits the clients needs, and right now its Ubuntu, virtualization, and hosted apps.  If MS can actually innovate for once, we will look at their options too if they price them right.

Just my opinion ;)

Shane Menshik
D2 GLOBAL INC
[http://www.d2global.com](http://www.d2global.com)

### Post by djsephiroth on 2009-02-12
> **the8thstar said:**

I don't know if Linux is the "future", more than Windows really. But it sure is a nice and flexible alternative. My opinion: use both if you can, this way, you'll win twice.
> **madjr said:**
> Just a word of [COLOR="Red"]**caution**[/COLOR]
also, don't make purchases with **credit cards or paypal accounts**.
:confused:
There's nothing inherently dangerous about either one of those activities. There's also nothing Windows-specific about the potential danger. Just pay attention and use common sense. Check for the "https", use and check keys and certificates, etc. I've done tens of thousands of dollars worth of business over teh internets using Paypal and credit cards, and never had an issue; irl, I've been given a fake check before.
>   
check **usb sticks** for **viruses** every time. But some viruses can't be detected or cleaned...

Malware doesn't mystically appear from the ether. I don't run regular virus scans on my flash drives because I know where they've been and I know what's on them.
> 
try not to **download** files/attachments, many have undetectable trojans horses.

Better yet, use common sense and only open attachments the origins of which you know and the contents of which are not particularly malware-prone.
> 
At least on every boot you should check if any **weird/suspicious processes are running in the background** and kill them. They could be sending personal info to the net, etc. Many times they are cleverly disguised.

Oh and **antiviruses/spyware removers may from time to time [COLOR="Red"]insert[/COLOR] Viruses and spyware in your system** ([COLOR="Sienna"]**this not a joke**[/COLOR])  they usually insert them so you **keep it** and say *"WOW this antivirus found a ton of viruses the "other brand" didn't"*. **It's a cruel business, they create many viruses** so they can keep you using their software.

Better yet, use common sense: don't install software if you don't know what it does. Use a limited account, and don't log in as an admin unless you have to.  

> **karellen said:**
> an OS is a tool, not a credo or ideology.
Well said. Er, typed. You know what I mean.
> 
but one thing I agree is that open source programs have very high quality/price ratio :)

### Post by solwic on 2009-02-13
> **Bonsanto said:**
> THings WHy I will leave Ubuntu If I don't get help fast.

1.- My system crashes.
2.- I got many "initramfs" bugs in the start of ubuntu.

6.- I get many problems when I try to install something. Becouse If it crashes It makes Ubuntu delete a "lib" things, So I have to reinsall all the OS again (I have done 9 times).
7.- I have been looking for the "crash solution" for 1 week and nothing was found.
8.- In WIndows XP, my system dosen't crash, I can leave my pc turned on for weeks and no crash. But in Ubuntu 2 programs = crash.
9.- People said "try with no acpi" I don't know where to type that comand, is too much genereic isn't especific.

It is a common theme among many religions and cultures that if you want help you should start by helping yourself.  Post your issues as *questions*, not demands, and then hit Google and try to fix the problem yourself.  

I have seen many posts like [this]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=1068460") one (which I started just last night) where the OP posts a question and then updates his own post with the answer.  I was the only person to post to that thread, but the problem was solved, and now maybe somebody sees that thread when they're trying to do the same thing and then voila!  I helped myself, shared the experience, which helped someone else help themselves.  

Everybody wins.  :)

Not only will you be a better person for doing all of this, but the people here on the forum won't give you #$@! for "demanding" things from them, and in the process of fixing your problems you will learn more about Linux, Ubuntu, and PC's in general...all of which make solving your *next* problem (and I assure you there will be another...with PC's there always is) that much easier. 

Good luck.  :)

### Post by old_codger on 2009-02-19
TBH sounds more like hardware problems that aren't showing up in windows for the moment. Bear in mind that if you're dual-booting you're not using the same part of the hard disk for windows as linux so maybe there are problems on the part of the disk with linux on?

Just a thought!

I had a similar problem with a PC a couple of years back and couldn't figure out what was going wrong. A low-level hard disk scan, *using UBCD or similar, IIRC), showed the problem.

Unfortunately I'd then wasted installing windows stuff only for them to start failing because I was then using that part of the disk as well.

If you'd like to copy some entries from the system log in /var/log/messages that occur just before it fails we can probably make some headway.

If you're just here to whinge... well... it's all good :)

The Codgemeister

### Post by jaminthorns on 2009-02-20
Why do people always complain about linux not "working out of the box?" The only reason Windows works flawlessly when you turn your PC on for the first time is because the manufacturers designed their components around Windows and had all the needed drivers preinstalled. If you use one of the new Dells that have Ubuntu preinstalled I GUARANTEE you it will be just as flawless of an experience.
   There is one, universal disk image for Ubuntu that is installed on all systems in exactly the same way that has to account for almost all consumer computers. Of course Ubuntu won't work flawlessly on your computer. Although, I'm still impressed by how well it does with finding correct drivers and indentification of hardware. I'm sure Windows couldn't do nearly as good of a job if it was installed on a system initially designed for another OS.

If you use one of the new Dells that have Ubuntu preinstalled I GUARANTEE you it will be just as flawless of an experience.
   There is one, universal disk image for Ubuntu that is installed on all systems in exactly the same way that has to account for almost all consumer computers. After countless installs of Windows in all flavor, let me ask you one thing, when does Windows work out of the box, heck, for everything you need drivers or else, Windows is crippled, it does come with a huge database of drivers but then again, unless one puts the latest drivers, performance suffers. Also Windows doesn't even come with basic word processor or spell check, even for that one has to shell out.

OTOH, Linux comes with everything installed, no need for drivers and if needed, its handled nicely by the repos, same goes for all the updates for software installed. In fact, Linux is perfectly install and forget OS, no worry of viruses, no need for AV, HIPS etc. Perfect for a non tech savvy person, the aged, housemakers etc. Install it once by an expert and then forget it.

### Post by Faolan on 2009-02-21
I left Linux for several reasons over the years. The last attempt was with SuSE before Novell bought them out. I have used RH in a Server Farm but that was just basic maintenance.

These are my thoughts:

Linux is a pretty good OS and generally works out of the box. However there is still too many rough edges to be truly acceptable to a home user. I'm a IT professional and I've had several problems with the installs, sure some of these tasks are not what a home user will do.

SAMBA and file sharing I spent three days trying to get SAMBA to play nicely with Vista x64. Yes I knew about the SecPol option but this isn't acceptable to me as I wanted a more secure LAN based around NTLMv2. SAMBA wouldn't allow Vista to connect despite being enable for that client. In the end it worked. However when I a share a folder on Win 2k, Server 2k3 or XP it works with Vista out of the box no hassles.

Opera won't connect to the 'Net, I've not figured this one out yet. Everything else on the boxes work but... Again another issue.

Choice and lack of clear purpose. Tracker Vs Beagle, Opera Vs Firefox and so on. There's an abundance of applications out here but little reviews on the software available and why each application is better suited to certain tasks.

Confusing user interface. This is one I'd hope that Linux would have solved but they haven't. Yes it's better, in fact it's a lot better. This is where things are generally better under Windows ie control panel. Even that's not perfect but at least when you want to change graphics or some such thing it's under or linked from one applet. Linux it's four (or more).

Applications.Sometimes when you install applications from Synapse it doesn't create a menu option or how to get to it. Lack of application coherency mainly aimed at the lack of a graphical suite for a better word for a professional workflow. Adobe Creative Suite has this market sewn up, but Linux really lacks the ability to integrate workflows to this degree. Even as a photographer the workflow from say Bridge to PS, or even Lightroom to PS is a compelling reason to stay on Windows. In some ways there's such a thing as too much choice.

Speed. People complain about Vista being slow, but it's not really any slower than Ubuntu in a everyday environ. In fact some features of Vista makes it a better environ to work under. The search function for apps is pretty quick and makes life a lot easier to find apps and docs. AWN is pretty good for Ubuntu but the hassles of setting up and figuring out how to optimise things... Plus AWN doesn't like either ATI or Nvidia graphics cards I get weird corruption every so often.

File systems. It's directories and configuration files spread across the drive. In Windows all you have really is program files, user directory and the registry. Under Linux it's a confusing mess for anybody not used to it. 

Adding hard drives and partitioning. I spent a day trying to get Linux to partition, mount and work with a SCSI HD. This isn't new tech but come on it shouldn't be this hard. Windows sees the drive you can go into disktools and partition/format and it works. Linux you have to partition, format, mount, and then edit the fstab! If there was a better/easier way I must have missed it. If I was a new user this would have me going back to Windows. Yes it's a rare occurrence but still...

CLI. There's is still too much dependence on getting your hands dirty to make things work. Also I've come across the arrogance of we don't want another Windows GUI box. Yes this is the great advantage of Linux but it's also one of it's greatest weaknesses. People don't want to be playing with a conf file just to get something to work. The average user is intimidated by a computer and telling them to go to the command line is akin to telling them to walk along the motorway. They just won't do it. There's a reason DOS was left behind and it wasn't just because it couldn't handle multitasking...

This may seem like a negative post but it's points I've noticed over the last week or so that I've been using Linux. It's been 2 years since I last tried and Linux has come a long way but it's still not ready as a desktop. Ubuntu has done a lot to give a cohesive feel but step out of the 'comfort' zone and install apps then you're on your own.

---

### Post by paradigm2 on 2009-04-04
> **helloyo said:**
> I’m writing this thread to show how someone like you moved from being exclusively Linux for years to being exclusively Microsoft. This isn’t because I love Microsoft, or hate Linux, but because I assessed my option and chose which one suited me most. 

A quick bit of history: I started using Linux with Red Hat 9 and was exclusively Linux not long after. Since then I have tried every major distribution and some of the smaller ones. Where I remained for the last couple years was on Ubuntu due to its large user base, professional releases and support. 

Recently I realised I was being too fundamentalist about many things in my life. I was very much set in my ways and was not open to different options. I discovered that I was doing this and made a lot of changes. Then I realised that I hadn’t given Windows a chance in many years. Linux was great and had been a lot of fun, but I wanted to spend more time using my computer than tinkering with it. 

So I took the plunge, wiped Linux and installed my old copy of Windows XP for a trial. Were there issues? Absolutely, but as a Linux user they were no problem to solve. Not only that, but learning the quirks wouldn’t benefit me for one 6 month release cycle, but for several years and on countless computers. I had become clueless about the vast majority of computer systems out there and needed to relearn a lot of it. 

So after years I played around on the seven year old Windows XP. I installed Windows versions of Firefox, Skype, Flash and Opera. They all worked better. Firefox supported more plugins, Skype was a generation ahead, Flash didn’t have Z-Index issues and Opera didn’t have a mismatched menu bar. I then installed software (trials) that I hadn’t used in years: Office 2007, Photoshop CS3 and some games. Their capabilities were phenomenal. Of course they’re not free and cost a fortune to produce, but that’s the point. They are a better product for a higher price. 

I soon took the plunge and bought Vista. It sure isn’t perfect, but it is still fantastic due to what it enables me to do. I can still support free software, but when it is advantageous for me to do it. I use Firefox because it is better, not because it is open source. That is also why I use Office 2007 over OpenOffice.org. 

 I love computers and marvelling at the new things that software can do. I used to think that the best way to pursue this hobby was with Linux but now I’m not so sure. Just playing around with the free, integrated voice recognition in Vista demonstrates that.

Linux is a massive testament to collaboration and what it can achieve. All I’m saying is keep your options open. Sure it costs more money, but considering how much time some of us spend on our computers it may be worth the investment.

I have to admit that Ubuntu can still take some work for the installation portion.  Then again so would windows if the windows programers and to mostly make all of their own drivers.

As a computing professional (I work over the internet), I am on my computer for long periods of time and it is the tool of my trade.  Also I am what would e called an "extreme power user" or perhaps pushingita little, an "expert user." Previously I had Windows XP home.  I found its limitations to be extremely frustrating.  In order to share my hard drives between computers SECURELY for example, I had to make use of a ssh tunnel.  So I had to find a ssh server for windows xp that actually worked.  Surprise, most of the ones I tried relied on cygwin (essentially emulates unix).  Either that or they cost $40 or they were just too buggy and insecure to use.  Finally I got an OLD version of copssh to work.  But it had problems too on windows, only connecting half the time and often crashing.  Such was the common story for many of the special network applications i wanted to make use of.  At one point I had three or four different programs installed on XP which had to make use of cygwin.

Finally I thought about it and decided, why not just switch over to *nix again?  The only thing I could think of was one single application which while convenient for me, is hardly a necessity.  There is currently no *nix replacement for it and it doesn't work on wine.

Days later now I cannot tell you how much happier I am that I made the switch to the Ubuntu Jaunty Beta!  For starters everything is so much faster!  I'm talking at least twice as fast.  I can do my work (which might involve having 12 tabs open at once in Firefox while running Opera with another seven tas open) without any lagging at all on my laptop!  And I don't need to hack around piecing things together so that they will run on Microsoft's Windows anymore either (e.g. the Polipo proxy, coppssh).  Not to mention that on my desktop the WMP110 v1.0 linksys wireless adapter works PERFECTLY right after the setup.  I didn't even have to touch a thing. Even on windows with the drivers provided for linksys it didn't work!

In summary while Linux has its problems (see my thread regarding having to do a tftp network boot installation on my laptop since it could never read the install CD correctly), overall I find Ubuntu to be far superior and more powerful for me.  Not to mention in some instances even EASIER to use.

Good luck with Vista.

### Post by jdunn on 2009-04-06
I have an HP Pavilion Laptop set up to dual boot Vista 64-bit and Ubuntu 8.10 64-bit.  Both work fine but I prefer using linux.  I'm a bit paranoid with Vista and have to run Komoto firewall, Avira antivirus and Windows Defender.  I know my way around Vista pretty well, but I'm still more comfortable with Unbuntu because I have better control over applications, processes, etc and I know what's going on.

Furthermore, The basic Vista install takes up roughly 40GB of drive space while Ubuntu only takes up around 3-4 GB.  Out of a total 4GB of physical memory, Vista 64bit uses about 1.3 GB at startup and Ubuntu 64bit only uses about 450 MB. Vista is a resource pig.

Ubuntu has this forum for support...It is extremely helpful.  What does Microsoft have?  Vista and XP home users have to fork over $$ for every phone call to tech support.

Having waited years for Vista (aka Longhorn) it was initially a disappointment when it finally arrived.  It is even more disappointing that just 2 years later, Windows 7 is already being hyped as what Vista should have been.

### Post by iKevin09 on 2009-05-31
I agree with the vast majority of the people on here. Linux isn't for everyone. Windows isn't for everyone, Mac isn't for everyone. Solaris isn't for everyone.

Windows: A lot of applications, huge support base, invest in an anti-virus

Mac: Somewhat a lot of applications, somewhat huge support base, no need for an anti-virus

Linux: a lot of 3rd party applications, huge support base, no need for an anti-birus

Solaris: a lot of 3rd party apps, small-medium support base, no need for anti-virus.

Granted, I know these three things aren't the only things I look for in an OS, there are a lot of other factors. I'm just saying organize what you want, do some homework and research the OS's that you are considering and install that OS that is suited for you.

---

### Post by Frak on 2009-06-23
> **hashan17 said:**
> Guys I don't think that it's not alway money.. 
Imagine that you have a ride (Mitsubhishi Lancer). You bought it for 96'000$ and now you have fixed a new sporty front/rear bumpers, leather seats, gauges, and all new alloy wheales... Does people from Mitsubhishi come and take back your car or go against courts, for modifying the car???? No they don't!!! They won't say anything even if you replace the engine!!! ;-) 

Can you do the same thing with your windows XP cd? Can you modify code?
Can you install it  on your notebook and the PC???
Is it legal? 

I think we should think beyond money. It's humanity guys... Jst helping people to share knowladge, help them to get up on their own. 

Knowladge belongs to the world... Let them have it!!!
At the same time, by knowing the parts of the car, you can't materialize parts out of thing air and duplicate the producation process of Mitsubishi. At the same time, by knowing how the car works doesn't mean you can do anything malicious to either Mitsubishi or their customers. Finally, they can't stop you from knowing how the car works because it's a physical object. Code is not a physical object, and is in fact just a human readable medium that later becomes strung out into complicated binary code. It is, indeed, legal to study and modify the Microsoft binary code as long as you don't redistribute it. Well, why would it be illegal to redistribute it you say? Thing that seperates physical objects from virtual is that you are able to duplicate the program you send, which is lost revenue to Microsoft. You cannot duplicate a car without costing you something in the process. If you were able to duplicate a Mitsubishi and give it away with a few modifications, you would be sued by Mitsubishi (under United States law). The inner workings of how Mitsubishi's products work is held under Intellectual Property and is subject to damages on a third party attempting to duplicate these designed inner workings. Moreover, some of the inner workings may be patented. If you duplicate these, no matter the country you live in, you will be sued. Patents are active in most of the countries in the world.

But again, you can study the workings of Microsoft's products, document them, spread them as far as you want, and you will not be sued. Learning something about a product to see how it works is not against the law, in fact, Fair Use protects this right. The knowledge gained from viewing the inner workings, documenting them, and then spreading it to a third party who has not agreed to the licensing on a product may use these to reverse engineer a solution to the problem. This is how we got our modern day BIOS chips and how Wine and ReactOS came to be. The law (in the United States at least) protects a person's right to learn from a source code. The law does not give you the right to duplicate another person's product unfairly and thus profit from it. The Fair Use law creates a loophole in which others may contest other companies with an alternative. Creating a duplicate Mitsubishi while studying a Mitsubishi with the intent to sale is against the law, but if you studied it and passed the information to a third party who has never studied a Mitsubishi to create a clone, you have taken the loophole approach to have a legal item.

And if any of you want to rip on Microsoft for being a company, do so, but they are a company and they're are obligated to do whatever it takes to rub out the competition. But, if you want to support anti-monopoly, then take your pick at either Linux, Mac OS X, BSD, etc. It is the consumers job to prevent a monopoly, not the monopoly.

This has just sat on my mind for a while, and I thought I'd post it. If you want to pick it apart and rip on it, I don't care. I'm just posting my view on the subject.

---

### Post by mdsmedia on 2009-06-24
Frak,

You've dug into one part of the law (IP and Fair Use) while ignoring another part of the law.

You say, and I'll paraphrase, that it's the duty of a monopoly to keep that monopoly, and stamp out the competition in any way possible.

There is a thing called anti-trust, in the US, and in Europe and in Australia, which is designed to prevent monopolies from using their monopoly status to unfairly hinder competition.

I don't criticise MS for being a company, with all the fiduciary responsibility that entails, but for using their monopoly status to stamp out competition in any way they can.

Most MS apologists complain that the EC, for example, are picking on MS and that MS has the right to do what they like with their OS, and this is true to a point, but where it unfairly hinders competition is what MS apologists ignore. Generally American MS apologists point at the EC picking on their American capitalistic values, while ignoring the point that monopolies are anti-competitive and what makes capitalism great is competition, so monopolies are, in essence, anti-capitalist and anti-free market.

---

### Post by Frak on 2009-06-24
> **mdsmedia said:**
> There is a thing called anti-trust, in the US, and in Europe and in Australia, which is designed to prevent monopolies from using their monopoly status to unfairly hinder competition.

I am very well aware of anti-trust. Microsoft has been in its fair share of AT cases. Though, the government is finding it hard to regulate a company that is so well welcomed into the industry. What if they split Microsoft up into individual parts like they did to AT&T? (AT&T provided much of the Fibre lines, and this development slowed when they were split) Due to the boom in eCommerce, and the boom in electronics use, it seems fragile to displace Microsoft from their current position of providing most of the software needed to have these processes performed. This, from what I know of, is the United States standpoint of it. They feel it is too big of a risk to chop up a business of their magnitude into smaller parts. There's an uneasy feeling of competition in software being a risky expenditure that may cause careless mistakes between companies due to rapid competition and thus leading to a possible partial collapse of the industry. While the computing industry is thriving and providing as much taxes as needed, the Government doesn't want to tilt the complex on its side just yet. If anything, there's a fear of another 80's Gaming crash occurring.

---

### Post by mdsmedia on 2009-06-25

There's an uneasy feeling of competition in software being a risky expenditure that may cause careless mistakes between companies due to rapid competition and thus leading to a possible partial collapse of the industry. If anything, there's a fear of another 80's Gaming crash occurring.
Why do you think that break up is the only remedy to anti-trust violations?

Simply regulating the behaviour of a company in a monopoly situation and providing remedies to violations of the laws should be sufficient, without breakup being the only remedy. 

What I was merely saying was that there is more to desired corporate behaviour than simply providing profits to your shareholders. Other laws which ARE in place, other than the fiduciary duty of directors to earn a profit for their investors. Microsoft has been in breach of those laws in a number of juristictions, and simply justifying their behaviour by the goal of returning profits to investors doesn't excuse it.
